SUBCOMMITTEE ON INTERIOR, ENVIRONMENT, AND RELATED AGENCIES

MICHAEL K. SIMPSON, Idaho, Chairman

MARK E. AMODEI, Nevada               CHELLIE PINGREE, Maine,
GUY RESCHENTHALER, Pennsylvania          Ranking Member
MICHAEL CLOUD, Texas                 BETTY McCOLLUM, Minnesota

DEPARTMENT OF THE INTERIOR, ENVIRONMENT, AND RELATED AGENCIES
APPROPRIATIONS FOR 2025

----------

Tuesday, April 16, 2024.

FISCAL YEAR 2025 REQUEST FOR THE U.S. FOREST SERVICE

WITNESSES

RANDY MOORE, CHIEF, U.S. FOREST SERVICE
MARK LICHTENSTEIN, NATIONAL BUDGET DIRECTOR, U.S. FOREST SERVICE
Mr. Simpson. The committee will come to order.
Good morning and thank you, Chief Moore, for being here
today to testify on the Forest Service fiscal year 2025 budget
request.
At last year's hearing, we were lucky to be joined by a
bird of prey that frequents our national forests, the bald
eagle. I think we all enjoyed hearing from the Peregrine Fund
about the work they are doing to conserve bald eagles and other
critical species. As a lifelong Idahoan, I have seen firsthand
how catastrophic wildfires can affect these birds and their
habitats, much like the devastating toll fires take on
communities across the country and other natural resources and
infrastructure. I have also seen where preventative measures
and sound management practices dramatically decreased the cost
of fire season, both in terms of taxpayer dollars and lives and
property loss. We must give our forest managers the tools and
authority to properly manage our Federal lands. Given these
widespread impacts, I am sure wildfires, their root causes, and
forest management practices will be the focus of the members'
questions today.
For fiscal year 2025, the President's request asked for
$6.5 billion for the Forest Service, an increase of $458
million above the fiscal year 2024-enacted level and,
approximately, a 7.5 percent increase. Notably, the budget
proposes shifting over $1.3 billion for wildfire preparedness,
suppression, and salaries from brace discretionary to emergency
funding. I have strong concerns about repeating the budgetary
gimmicks that Congress has just rectified in the fiscal year
2024 bill, so I look forward to discussing the Forest Service
decision to propose this change.
I was glad to see the Forest Service budget continues to
prioritize compensation for wildland firefighters, wildland
fire risk management, and access to all the resources our
public lands have to offer. As many of my colleagues may know,
the infrastructure bill provided a base pay supplemental to
wildland firefighters. Since those funds have run out, the
uncertainty of competitive pay has created challenges in hiring
and maintaining a Federal firefighter workforce. In 2024, this
committee fought hard to provide funding and authority for the
Forest Service to continue firefighter pay at the level
provided by the infrastructure bill. Forest Service and
Department of Interior wildland firefighters risk their lives
to protect our communities and deserve the stability of a
permanent pay reform. I am pleased the administration
recognizes the important work of wildland firefighters and is
continuing its efforts to address these issues.
Before closing, I would like to note that I generally
enjoy, and I don't just say this because you are here, not
generally, but genuinely enjoy working with Forest Service
employees in Idaho's region and across the country. I value the
important jobs that they carry out, and I have really enjoyed
working with them. They are trying to do a job that is
sometimes a very tough job because everybody in this country
knows that they could manage forests better than the Forest
Service can. It is the Monday morning quarterback sort of
thing. The Forest Service employees are trying to do a
sometimes difficult job, and I appreciate the work they do.
Your leadership at the Forest Service is vital to our public
lands and communities across the country. Thank you again for
joining us this morning. I look forward to our discussion and
continuing to work with you. Now I would like to yield to my
ranking member, Ms. Pingree, for her opening statement.
Ms. Pingree. Thank you very much, Mr. Chair, and thank you
so much for being here with us today. Chief Moore, thank you.
We are very pleased to have a chance to talk with you and hear
your thoughts on the fiscal 2025 budget request for the Forest
Service. I really appreciated our most recent call. Thank you
for taking the time to go over a few things with me. I know you
are very aware that I come from the most forested State in the
Nation. We are very proud of our forests in Maine, but we are
also well aware of the challenges that we are facing, from
invasive species to unpredictable weather, economic issues. We
have really been supportive of all of the things, which you
have also, around wood innovations, everything from biochar to
wood fiber insulation. I look forward to getting you to visit
our State sometime so you can see some of the work we are
doing, but I really appreciate your investment and support for
innovations in our forests and things that can be applied
across the country.
It is kind of hard to believe that it took us until last
month to finish the fiscal 2024, but I am very glad that we are
now starting 2025. We were forced to make some difficult
reductions in the Interior bill, and I understand that those
cuts will have impacts to the Forest Service programs. Your
budget also takes a constrained approach to stay within the
budget cap, so I hope that during today's hearing, you will
help us to better understand what resources are necessary to
ensure the Forest Service is able to meet its mission.
The administration's commitment to supporting wildland
firefighters and their families is commendable. Your budget
continues and expands the pay reforms that were first funded by
the Bipartisan Infrastructure Law. Providing equitable
competitive compensation, as we all know, is essential to
improving firefighter recruitment and retention. In the
recently-enacted bill, we provided additional funding necessary
to continue pay supplements for firefighters, but
unfortunately, the language authorizing pay reforms was not
included, so I hope that we can make more progress on that in
the fiscal year 2025. I look forward to discussing these and
other topics with you. Thank you again for being with us today,
and I yield back.
Mr. Simpson. Thank you, Ranking Member Pingree. With that,
Chief Moore, you may proceed with your opening statement. Your
official submitted testimony will be included in the record.
Mr. Moore. Thank you. Chair Simpson, Ranking Member
Pingree, and members of the committee, thank you for inviting
me to testify today. We are grateful for your continued
support.
The President's budget names three primary goals for us:
one, to modernize wildland fire management, to sustain
investments critical to our mission, and ensure equitable
access and benefits to America. Today, I will share our
progress as we put money to work to confront serious
challenges. I will share how our work will continue to be a
sound investment.
We directly stood 193 million acres of public lands. We
reach across boundaries to assist States, Tribes, communities,
private landowners, and others to keep millions more acres
healthy and productive. Together, these lands provide basic
needs for life, clean air and water, while they contribute to
energy production and support local communities.
National forests alone contribute more than 410,000 jobs
and $44.3 billion to the GDP. To sustain productivity and
health, forests must be able to withstand threats posed by
wildfire, climate change, drought, disease, and insects. We
invested resources to act and ensure that they do just that.
Foremost, we prioritize work to reduce wildfire risk, safeguard
communities, and create healthy and resilient forests.
In 2022, we launched a 10-year wildfire crisis strategy,
and we moved to implement it. Annual appropriations, coupled
with the historic Bipartisan Infrastructure Law and the
Inflation Reduction Act, provided an extraordinary opportunity
to take bold and strategic actions. We did just that. We
progressed to deliver on a promise to increase pace and scale
of our treatments. We are not just treating any acres. We are
treating the right acres in the right place and at the right
scale. We focused additional efforts on 21 priority landscapes
within Western fire sheds that propose the highest risk, and
they account for roughly 80 percent of wildfire risk.
These investments are paying dividends. Experts, basically,
our scientists, have reported roughly $700 billion worth of
housing and infrastructure that are at risk within these 21
priority landscapes. This includes about $6.5 billion of
municipal watersheds, which supply drinking water to 12 million
people. In the last 2 years, we reduced, on average, wildfire
risk to these assets by 8 percent for infrastructure, 8 percent
for housing, and 12 percent for watersheds. That means we
protected more than $175 million worth of homes that were at
risk. In the Stanislaus National Forest, for example,
communities are at lower risk, including towns of Cold Springs
and Strawberry. A million socially-vulnerable people are less
at risk. Nearly half of national forest lands and priority
landscapes are now considered unlikely to burn at high
intensity. We must remain on course and build on these gains.
We recognize the urgency of investing in a permanent and
comprehensive pay increase to provide a more livable wage,
enhance recruitment, and stabilize retention. We must also
improve housing conditions and provide better care for our
firefighters' physical and mental health. We look forward to
seeing an end to reports of firefighters living in cars with
few benefits and limited mental healthcare. Visitor use,
hunting and fishing, energy and minerals development, forest
products and livestock grazing generate 69 percent of the
contributions to the economy. Thanks to the great American
Outdoor Ag funds will relieve some of the pressure from the
$8.6 billion maintenance backlog. The budget requests $58
million to maintain critical recreation services with a focus
on offering welcoming and equitable opportunities.
We are also making a difference in our urban environment.
The Forest Service and partners are planting and maintaining
trees in cities and suburbs where about 84 percent of Americans
live. Trees combat extreme heat and climate change and improve
access to nature. The 2025 budget returns basic funding to most
programs. This includes forest products, which are vital to
sustaining rural communities. This includes sustainable timber
supply. We know it is a critical component and is part of a
complex, market-driven system. While we don't control markets,
we can support industry through forest products and wood
innovations, which help mills adapt and modernize.
In closing, we are fully committed to meeting the
challenges before us with resources that Congress has provided.
The people of America deserve nothing less than to see their
money put to work to benefit all. Thank you, and I welcome your
questions.
[The statement of Mr. Moore follows:]
[GRAPHIC(S) NOT AVAILABLE IN TIFF FORMAT]

Mr. Simpson. Thank you, and let me say this is kind of one
of those unusual years, and seem to be more frequent than in
the past, but because of the shortened hearing schedule that we
have on all of our subcommittees, there are multiple
subcommittees meeting, and, of course, most members serve on
two or three. Mr. Amodei has to go chair his Homeland Security
Committee here at 10 o'clock and others have other meetings
that they are currently at and will be stepping in and out. It
is not due to a lack of respect or anything----
Mr. Moore. Sure.
Mr. Simpson [continuing]. For the Forest Service and the
job that you all do, but it is kind of crazy the way things are
going, the time period we are trying to get, because we are
actually trying to get a budget to you, dare I say, before
October 1. That is kind of a goal sort of thing. [Laughter.]
Mr. Simpson. I am tempted to ask how the 2024 budget is
working out, but that is kind of an unfair question because you
just barely got it. Anyway, let me ask a couple of questions
here. First, thank you and your team for the work that you have
put in to advance the Stibnite Mine Project in Idaho.
Mr. Moore. Yeah.
Mr. Simpson. This project is essential to ensuring the
United States has a domestic supply of antimony for national
defense purposes. In fact, some of the biggest proponents of
this moving along is our military. As we all know, in many
supply chains, we continue to be far too reliant on foreign
adversaries for critical minerals. It is important that the
Stibnite mines stay on schedule to meet the national security
demands that we are facing today.
When I visited the project, I was very impressed by how the
company is prioritizing restoration of the historic abandoned
mine, while simultaneously producing a desperately-needed
critical mineral for our national defense. I have to compliment
the Forest Service for the work that they have done there and
the cooperation that they have had. Have you had the
opportunity to visit the Stibnite Mine?
Mr. Moore. No, I have not.
Mr. Simpson. If you would like to come, I would be happy to
take you up there, and we would get away from all the
cellphones and all that kind of stuff.
Mr. Moore. I would love to take you up on that.
Mr. Simpson. It is a neat place.
Mr. Moore. Yeah.
Mr. Simpson. Let's talk for a minute, if we could, about
addressing the wildfire pay. It continues to be one of my, and
I think this committee's, top priorities. The President's
fiscal year 2025 budget includes a proposal to reform
firefighter pay with a permanent pay increase. As I previously
mentioned, this committee fought hard to provide additional
funding and authority in the 2024 bill for the Forest Service
to ensure the Federal Agency's ability to recruit and retain
wildland firefighters temporarily, but I recognize a permanent
fix is needed. What are the implications if Congress does not
enact a permanent pay solution for wildfire fighters? Along
that same line, what is the cost difference between the amount
needed to sustain the infrastructure bill's supplemental
payments and the amount needed to implement the permanent
salary table included in the request?
Mr. Moore. Thank you, Chairman Simpson. Let me respond to
your Stibnite question first. You may or may not be aware that
we plan to have a draft completed in the summer or fall of this
year, and we also are planning to have a final release toward
the end of this year. A lot of progress on the analysis for
that program.
In terms of the fire pay fix, it is a real challenge for
us. In many of our communities, and I know we feel that all
across America where it is getting more expensive to live in so
many of our communities. When you have firefighters who are
paid at the lower end of the Federal scale, it is really hard
for them to make a living on the previous amount, and so the
supplemental fund has been a real boost because at one time, we
were having a lot of bleeding in the organization of employees
leaving. The retention was low. We had a lot of declinations.
That has basically stopped just on the ideal that Congress is
considering a permanent fix.
Mr. Simpson. Yeah.
Mr. Moore. Now, if for some reason the fix does not go
through, we will continue to see what we were seeing prior to
the supplement. As we have looked at some of the analysis
there, the permanent pay fix is actually cheaper than the
temporary supplemental fix that we have now. It is in all of
our best interest to have a permanent pay fixture for a lot of
different reasons, one of those being is cheaper.
Mr. Simpson. Yeah. That kind of floored me when my staff
told me that, that it is cheaper to actually do a permanent pay
fix than the supplemental that we are currently doing is. How
is the recruiting for the upcoming fire season? Is it going
okay?
Mr. Moore. Yes. So far, the recruitment is going good, but
like I say, it is tenuous. A lot of our firefighters are a
little nervous about what to expect, but so far it is going
fairly well, and so we are on schedule to meet our goals in
terms of the number of additional firefighters. The one thing
that people may not notice, though, is that we have about
13,000 other firefighters that are not classified as
firefighters but also support the program.
Mr. Simpson. Yeah.
Mr. Moore. They are employees that work in other program
areas like timber, recreation, some of those types of things.
Mr. Simpson. Could you give me a brief what is it like to
be a wildfire fighter, a year-long sort of thing because they
come and work as wildfire fighters for a period of time when
the wildfires are going on, and then they go back to other jobs
that they have.
Mr. Moore. Yeah, I think it is morphing a bit. I will go
back to when I started with the Forest Service and I worked as
a firefighter, and the work is still dirty, hard, and nasty.
When you are at the bottom of the pay scale it is not rewarding
until you get your check, of course.
Mr. Simpson. Yeah.
Mr. Moore. After that, it is really hard work. If I look at
firefighters today, it is still hard work. It is still dirty
work. It is still something that is really necessary for
America.
Mr. Simpson. Dangerous work.
Mr. Moore. Dangerous, very dangerous. We have seen too many
times where our employees have been in situations where the
outcome wasn't so good. It is imperative, I think, for us to
find ways to look at the pay fix, for one, on this, and I think
we are headed in the right direction. I want to applaud
Congress for all of their efforts and attention to this issue
for us, so much appreciation from me as chief of the Agency.
Mr. Simpson. Well, I appreciate that. As you know, I don't
think the debate has been about whether firefighters deserve
this pay increase that they got. It is about authorization and
not stepping on other committees' toes. That is what we are
working on, but this needs to be fixed on a permanent basis, so
I appreciate that. Ms. Pingree.
Ms. Pingree. I want to start with a very parochial kind of
question. I know we have a lot of issues to talk about today,
but this one impacting my own State. The State private and
Tribal forestry account provides vital financial and technical
assistance to non-Federal landowners and resource managers in
my home State. The Maine Forest Service has a long history of
using these resources to provide services to small woodland
owners, loggers, and foresters, but the State and private
funding only goes so far. In the last few months, Maine has
experienced four major storms that caused widespread water
quality issues, washed out roads and bridges, and caused
negative impacts to the health of the forests. Maine's logging
industry suffered more than $2.6 million in losses for just one
of those storms, the December storm alone, and has a domino
effect on the Maine economy, with larger losses estimated at
over $5.5 million. Many of the companies will be unable to file
insurance claim because the losses aren't covered. I have seen
some of the devastating pictures of road washouts, and it is a
short season when you can move trees. It is not mud, it is not
snowed in, so it has been a huge problem for them.
How is the Forest Service taking into consideration the
impact of this extreme weather we are experiencing on the
management of partners' forests and related industry, and is
providing more resources for this simply a matter of increasing
the funding to State, private, and Tribal forestry accounts, or
are there some other funding possibilities that a partner, like
the Maine Forest Service could explore? We are just anxious to
help them, and we want to know what your thoughts are and any
resources we should be looking at.
Mr. Moore. Sure. Ranking Member, first, let me say that we
can and we do request funding through the congressionally-
required disaster unfunded needs report that is provided, and
so we have received funding there. To give you an idea of the
request versus the need is pretty significant. We have about a
billion dollars right now in backlog from requests that are
needed to respond to many of these disasters across the
country.
Ms. Pingree. Mm-hmm.
Mr. Moore. The request is one thing. Do we get enough to
repair the damage? The answer is no, we don't, but it is
helpful. We want you to know how much we appreciate Congress'
response to our request. It is just that more of them are
happening, it seems, and whether you believe that it is from
climate differences, climate change, or whatever the reason may
be, it is happening more often, and so the requests are asked
more often. We understand the budget situation, we fully
understand that, but I do want you to know that there is a lot
of damage out there. We did receive the 2023 funding requests
for prior years, and it still wasn't enough. Like I said, we
have a billion-dollar backlog on what is needed versus what has
been requested.
Ms. Pingree. Great. That is helpful to know and sort of
gives us a sense of where that funding could come from. Let me
just throw in one other quick question. I am very interested in
agroforestry.
Mr. Moore. Yes.
Ms. Pingree. It is a funny thing about this committee
because I also sit on Agriculture, and this is kind of
agriculture, but it comes under forestry, and so I got to ask
you here, but it is already an important tool in farming to
mitigate some of the effects of climate change. It helps us
sequester more carbon in the soil. It just has a lot of
benefits, particularly in hot weather where animals are better
off with some shade, so just all kinds of good reason for it.
The National Agroforestry Center plays a critical role in
facilitating the best practices by providing cutting-edge
science, technical assistance, the things that farmers need to
be able to use this funding in this program.
Is the current national agroforestry staff able to meet the
demand for the latest research and technical assistance? What
staffing needs do you have at the Center? I will just say that
from my conversations that I have had with groups from around
the country and people who are interested in this in my State,
there is just not enough out there right now. I am sort of
saying what do we need to be able to provide the resources that
farmers need to use these tools?
Mr. Moore. Yeah. Let me first acknowledge the accuracy in
your statement. The Agroforestry Center provides really
valuable research, and it really does a good job of translating
scientific knowledge into a support base on how we can use
that. If we had additional resources, for example, our top
priorities would be around supporting agroforestry training
developing technical assistance material, and then promoting
and facilitating agroforestry organizations, agencies, and some
of the producers. Looking at how the allocations happen,
though, if we use more money for the Agroforestry Center, it
would also have a negative impact on some of the other research
that is taking place in research and development. What we have
in our budget now is a balanced approach based on what we have
within our research and development budget. I would agree with
you, there is some outstanding work taking place in the
Agrocenter, and we would like to do more.
Ms. Pingree. Great. Great. Well, thank you for that, and I
will yield back.
Mr. Simpson. Thank you. Mr. Ellzey has got to get back to
CJS----
Mr. Moore. Yeah.
Mr. Simpson [continuing]. Thank you, Congressman Cloud, for
allowing him to go first. Go ahead.
Mr. Ellzey. Thank you, Mr. Chairman, Ranking Member, and
thank you, Michael, for letting me cut in line. I am sure I
will pay for it later. [Laughter.]
Mr. Ellzey. Chief Moore and Mister Lichtenstein--the name I
love to pronounce over and over and over again----
[Laughter.]
Mr. Ellzey [continuing]. Texas is comprised of 63 million
acres of forested land, approximately one-third of the State.
It is the most forested State in the lower 48, which I really
didn't know, but doesn't surprise me, with 95 percent of that
land privately held, and the entire value chain of that land is
$4 billion. Of course, in February and March, earlier this
year, several wildfires broke out across the Texas Panhandle
region, where I am originally from. Strong winds caused power
lines to fall, and dry conditions allowed these fires to
spread, and it was largely grassland--it wasn't forested land
that burned--with the Smokehouse Creek fire damaging more than
1 million acres, in Windy Deuce, 150,000. Of course, these are
devastating losses to landowners, but by the grace of God,
truly were not more people killed. However, it killed a lot of
livestock, and it took that land out of graze land capability
for many years.
Of course, we lost two folks in those fires. I want to
commend Forest Service's Region 8 personnel, leadership, and
responders for their assistance in managing those wildfires.
Their assistance to Texas and local responders in capacity
building and wildfire suppression was critical in controlling
and extinguishing these fires.
Every 5 years or so, the Panhandle has these wildfires. The
winds can get extremely strong. There are no trees there, and
there is nothing to stop that wind. That is why I live in the
Dallas area now. [Laughter.]
The wind, 30 miles an hour every day, and when it gets dry,
it is just going to catch fire and blow fast. As the threat of
wildfires grows and the cost of damages increases, and the
utilization rate of a firefighting capacity in the aerial
regime is increasing and it has been increasingly critical. In
the Wildland Fire Mitigation and Management Commission's Aerial
Equipment Strategy report of January 2023, the report
recommends that DOD surplus equipment and parts should be made
more readily available to State and local wildfire agencies and
the private contractor wildland fire community. Now, I happen
to know that the Air Force is getting rid of their D.C.-10s
quite quickly, and they are going to go to the bone yard. Can
you elaborate on the DOD's surplus equipment and what you
envision this partnership with DOD looking like?
Mr. Moore. At this point, I cannot comment on what DOD is
planning to do with their equipment. I have not been briefed on
that. I can talk from my experience, particularly working in
California and also looking at the possibility of owning a lot
of the large air tankers in the past. As we have done some
analysis on what it would take to put that within our
portfolio, it is more expensive for us to own and operate that
aircraft than it is to contract out the aircraft.
We have chosen to support the business sector by
contracting out. We have two basic contract options, exclusive
use or call when needed, and so far those options are working
fairly well for us.
Mr. Ellzey. Okay. You basically described how you are
building a long-term fleet, and I completely agree with you. As
a contract air services air provider after I retired from the

providing a reliable domestic supply of one of the most
climate-friendly building products around.
The impacts on our national forests and on communities like

Mr. Moore. Okay. Thank you, Congressman. The first thing I

Some of the challenges, though, on how you had looked at

mass timber production, and we are looking at how do we
encourage our business community to invest in some of these
opportunities? Now, I said it looks promising because right
now, we have just under 2,000 facilities that are either
designed, being designed, or being built using cross-laminated
timber or what we call mass timber. We always want to talk
about one of the biggest successes, which is in Milwaukee. It
is a 26-story building using cross-laminated timber. We have a
few facilities here in the D.C. area. The market is taken off
and has proven itself.
The other thing, I met with a community yesterday that came
in to visit, and it was a community that was devastated by
fire. Looking at some of the science that we are working on
with some of the university systems, like in a forest products
laboratory, we are looking at material from biochar that you
can use for tile roofing on your house. Also, the same type of
material that you can begin to build homes and put walls in,
now that is more resistant to fire. The technology is being
really developed and refined, and so I am really optimistic
about that part of it looking into the future and how we
calculate what we are able to do differently, not just saw
logs, but some of the other material that comes from wood.

I wanted to touch on one thing, and that is controlled
burns. Flora and fauna have long lived alongside of wildfires.
There are a number of things that we see, fascinating ways
animals even adapt to what is going on with wildfires. Black
fire beetle has a pair of infrared-sensitive pits capable of
detecting wood burns a mile away. Spotted owl hunts animals
that are revealed after a wildfire. We see the black-backed
woodpecker feast on insects of trees that have been burned, and
we know that these wood burns are actually if we did nothing,
it is part of the natural life cycle of--I will spare you the
song--but the circle of life that would happen in wildfires.
Could you speak to the benefits of prescribed burns?
Mr. Moore. Yes. Actually, thank you for that question. When
I look at most of the landscapes across this country, and it
varies, the South is different than the West, and in some ways,
the South is maintaining ecosystems that they have created over
time with prescribed burning, whereas in the West, it needs to
create those conditions before we can begin to talk about
maintaining them.
To give you a kind of a difference between the two, in the
West, where you have the fire-adapted ecosystems and the fact
that we have not been able to go in and treat those acres at
the scale that it needs treating, then it has grown up. On
average, in the past, you had about, let's just say 40 to 60
trees per acre, particularly on the ponderosa pine, that type
of ecosystem. Now, you could have upwards of 600 trees or more
per acre on that same acre.
The fact that you have that much vegetation there, you
cannot introduce wildfire in those areas before you thin it,
and in order to thin it, and based on the type of material you
have, you need to have an industry that can utilize it, and it
helps to provide economic stability in a lot of our small rural
communities. It is a cycle that we have to look at if we want
to try and treat this issue in a way that there are multiple
benefits from it.
Now, the wood innovations approach is one of the ways we
are trying to help stimulate a new way of using the type of
material that we have, which is small diameter low value, and
so we are working with industry to look at different ways of
utilizing the type of material we have. We still will be
looking at saw logs and some of the large timber, but there is
so much more of the other type of material, and that is why I
mentioned some of the things that we are doing through wood
innovations and how promising it is looking.

Ms. Pingree. Sure. Well, thank you. I just want to go back

fix. This, I think, mostly is due to our chair, so thank you

really valuable. It does make additional funding for wildfire
response and suppression available when we don't have the
regular funding, but the budget authority, I guess, is set to

Mr. Moore. Sure. First of all, thank you for the Fire Fund
and fix. It has been a godsend, and I would like to explain
just briefly how. If I look at what happened between 2020 and
2023, we would have needed an additional $5 billion for fire
suppression that this has provided us. As I look at the 2025
budget, we would have to request $1.5 billion in it to address
it.
I think more than anything, the Fire Fund and fix provided
stability in the operational areas of the Agency because it was
taking money that was targeted for other resource areas to look
at fire suppression. The 10-year average, as you know, was
going up every year, and every year, the requests, the movement
from some of the resource programs into fire was just really
having a devastating impact on the other program areas within
the Agency. This Fire Fund and fix was not only necessary, but
it provided us with the stability that we needed to do some of
the things that we have been talking about here this morning.
If that was not renewed, then we will go back in time and look
at the impacts and implications that it would have on the other
parts of our mission, which would be devastating to the Agency
as well to the American public.

Mr. Moore. Yeah. I have had a lot of experience with forest
plan revision, and I also know that if left on its own, it will
take forever to do a plan. I will use the example when I was in
the Eastern Region where I had 12 plans to do, but I looked at
two or three other plans and it had taken 5 and 7 years, and it
still wasn't completed. That is costing American taxpayers
money because we always see a way to improve it, and so we
start back over because we find things that make it better, and
that is a continuous kind of cycle. I think for us, the Federal
Government, we need to get beyond that.
To give you an idea, and this is my thought process, I had
developed a 2-year plan in the eastern region, and I had to do
12 plans in 2 years. People they were mad for a while, of
course, but after that, do you know we developed a plan, a
strategy, and we did all 12 plans in 2 years. I know that we
can do these plans in a much more abbreviated fashion. The
question is that does the public feel like they have had enough
time to be engaged and involved in the process? That is why if
we bring our public to the table in the very beginning to have
these discussions about what we want in a forest plan, and once
that is described and identified, then we move to make it
happen rather than allowing things to continue to trickle in,
which delays the completion of a forest plan revision.
That is the approach that we want to take with the
Northwest Forest Plan. That is also the approach we want to
take with forest plan revisions across the country. We develop
plan teams. We have developed three across the country--the
west, the midwest, and the east--and their goal is to reduce
the impacts on individual forests so that we can look at
planning as serious business. We do it, we engage our publics,
and then we do the work that we all agreed to do.

Mr. Moore. No, we couldn't possibly wrap this up. I am not

Mr. Moore. I mean, trees do sequester carbon. Also, when

Mr. Simpson. Thank you. Just a couple quick questions
before we wrap this up.
The Forest Service plays a vital role in public/private
forest restoration initiatives around the country. In some
instances, industry partners have committed hundreds of
millions of dollars in capital investments to support and
achieve the goals of these collaborative landscape-scale
initiatives to restore forest health and reduce the threat of
catastrophic wildland fires. In more than one part of the
country, we have seen frustration and concern over the slow
pace of the Forest Service approving additional acreage for
treatment. The fact is that without NEPA-approved acres, these
partners will not be able to stay in business, causing the
shutdown of mills, the loss of jobs, and the undermining of the
National Strategy to prevent wildfires.
What are the biggest impediments to the Forest Service
approving additional NEPA-approved acres for treatment in
regions prone to catastrophic wildland fires? Is it largely a
funding issue, a personnel issue, and how can the Forest
Service step up to access the NEPA-approved acres for these
partners? What steps can Congress take to assist the Forest
Service in expediting the availability of NEPA-approved acres
for forest health restoration and thinning efforts in regions
prone to catastrophic wildfires?
Mr. Moore. Chair, it is a combination of all of those, and
what we have right now is really a balance based on the budget
and what we can afford, whether it is NEPA or whether it is
personnel. I will tell you some of the things that we have done
over the last few years to improve our efficiency and our
effectiveness of the program.
Now about 87 percent of our NEPA is categorical exclusion,
which is the lowest form of that, and so we have about 1,130 or
so categorical exclusion that we implemented this past year. We
have very few EAs, environmental assessment, versus
environmental impact statements, EIS. We are looking at trying
to utilize the appropriate tools for the work. We have
increased the use of CEs by 7 percent from just 3 years ago, so
we are looking at that.
The other thing, Secretary of Vilsack has given us
emergency authority, and this past year, we had about 37
projects, which includes about a million acres of work to do on
the ground. The thing about the emergency authorities is that
it reduces the amount of time for us to complete projects
anywhere between 4 to 6 months. We have been looking at a whole
range of tools to help us manage and to find the right tool to
do the work that we are being asked to do.
Mr. Simpson. One more question, and this one is generally
asked by Mr. Amodei. I am surprised you didn't ask it. His mind
must have been on Homeland Security stuff.
The 2023 Wildland Fire Mitigation and Management Commission
report found that a lack of housing is a significant barrier to
the recruitment, hiring, and retention of Federal wildland
firefighters. Many Forest Service duty stations are in areas
where housing costs and availability are beyond the economic
means of wildland firefighters, the majority of which are paid
at the GS-3 and GS-4 level. The President's budget requested
for the Forest Service included a request for funding
specifically to address this. Is there a role for building
materials, like mass timber that is fire resistant, that could
be sourced from our own forest to support this initiative?
Mr. Moore. Yes, there are. In fact, I have been doing show
and tells lately, and I didn't think to bring some of the
material that is being developed from our forest products
laboratory.
Mr. Simpson. We have got to train you to do that.
[Laughter.]
Mr. Moore. Appropriate, but I wanted to do that. In fact, I
have been having some of those discussions, and you are right
about lot of different communities. It is getting tougher and
tougher for our employees to live in many of our communities
where it is just expensive to live, and there seemed to be more
and more of that. You have heard these stories about
firefighters sleeping in cars----
Mr. Simpson. Yeah.
Mr. Moore [continuing]. They can't afford. You also looked
at some of our government housing as another option, and those
houses, the quarters are located out where the work needs to
take place. I want to get a really good feel for what our

[Laughter.]
Mr. Moore [continuing]. There is a lot of work that needs
to take place in many of our quarters. That is why we were

It is three-pronged, though. It is availability,

Wednesday, April 17, 2024.

FISCAL YEAR 2025 REQUEST FOR THE DEPARTMENT OF THE INTERIOR

WITNESSES

HON. DEB HAALAND, SECRETARY, U.S. DEPARTMENT OF THE INTERIOR
DENISE FLANAGAN, DIRECTOR OF BUDGET, U.S. DEPARTMENT OF THE INTERIOR
JOAN M. MOONEY, PRINCIPAL DEPUTY ASSISTANT SECRETARY, U.S. DEPARTMENT
OF THE INTERIOR

Opening Statement of Chairman Mike Simpson

Mr. Simpson. Good morning, and welcome to Secretary
Haaland. Thank you for joining us today to discuss the
President's fiscal year 2025 budget request for the Department
of Interior.
Nearly two-thirds of the State of Idaho consists of Federal
land, which means that land management decisions made by
Federal agencies like yours have a profound and direct impact
on our State's economy and Idahoans who live, work, and
recreate on or near Federal lands. Indeed, most Idahoans
interact with the Department and its bureaus daily. The
Department and this subcommittee also have the important
responsibility of fulfilling our treaty and trust obligations
to Tribes. Addressing the greatest Tribal needs, including
education, law enforcement, and Tribal land management issues,
continue to be a top priority of this subcommittee under both
Republican and Democratic chairs, and I am sure newly-elected
Chairman Cole shares this sentiment. I have talked to him about
it. I appreciate your active involvement working with us and
our American Indian and Alaska Native brothers and sisters to
address these issues in the fiscal year 2025 budget.
In terms of discretionary resources, the President's
request for fiscal year 2025 asked for over $16 billion for the
Department of Interior, an increase of $1.6 billion, or 11
percent, above the fiscal year-2024 enacted level. Notably, the
budget proposes shifting more than $830 million from base
discretionary to emergency funding for wildfire activities. As
I said yesterday to the chief of the Forest Service, I have
serious concerns about repeating the budgetary gimmicks that
were just rectified by Congress in the 2024 bill. While I may
not agree with all the priorities in this budget proposal or
the recent rulemaking put out by the Department, I appreciate
that we can have a productive conversation about the land
management issues we are facing and the tools you need to
manage our lands effectively and efficiently.
My colleagues and I hope to cover a lot of ground with you
today. Now, I would like to yield to my ranking member, Ms.
Pingree, for her opening statement.

Statement of Ranking Member Pingree

Ms. Pingree. Thank you very much, Mr. Chair, and thank you
so much, Secretary Haaland, for being with us today to discuss
the fiscal year 2025 budget request for the Department of
Interior. I continue to be so pleased that you bring your
perspective to this position as the Secretary and just always
happy to work with you.
Your budget continues to build on this administration's
commitment to addressing climate crisis. It strengthens the
Federal Government's response to wildland fire, and it ensures
that we are upholding our treaty and trust obligations. As the
steward of our Federal lands, your Agency faces significant
climate-related challenges from drought, wildfire, floods, and
invasive species, so I am pleased to see continued investments
to advance science and enhance conservation efforts. The budget
also seeks to meet our responsibilities and legal obligations
to protect Indian trust assets and resources and to provide
direct services such as education, public safety, and justice.
A total increase of $651 million is proposed for Indian affairs
with targeted investments to address missing and murdered
indigenous people, continue the Secretary's Boarding School
Initiative and its comprehensive review of Federal boarding
school policies, and to support native language revitalization.
I believe these increases are appropriate and necessary.
Secretary Haaland, thank you again for appearing before us
this morning. I appreciate your testimony and your answers to
our questions. I yield back, Mr. Chair.
Mr. Simpson. Thank you, Ranking member Pingree. Secretary
Haaland, you may proceed with your opening statement. Your
official submitted testimony will be included in the record.

Statement of Hon. Deb Haaland, Secretary, Department of the Interior

Secretary Haaland. Yes. Thank you. Chair Simpson, Ranking
Member Pingree, and members of the subcommittee, thank you for
the opportunity to testify in support of Interior's fiscal year
2025 budget request. I appreciate all of the support this
subcommittee and your staff have consistently shown the
Department of the Interior. We appreciate that. I have
especially enjoyed visiting so many of your home districts.
Every trip informs my understanding of the issues important to
the American people, the impact of the work that we do, and how
our budget can support those interests.
Our 2025 budget totals $18 billion in current authority. Of
that amount, $16.4 billion is within the jurisdiction of the
Interior Appropriations Subcommittee. First, I want to
highlight several important proposals: permanent pay
legislation and reforms for our wildland fire workforce,
mandatory funding for future Indian water rights settlements,
and reclassifying contract support costs and leasing payments
to Tribes from discretionary to mandatory funding starting in
2026.
This administration has made a steadfast commitment to
strengthen government-to-government relationships with Tribal
nations. We are doing so thanks to significant investments from
Congress, which are helping address the deficiencies that
decades of underfunding have created. I am grateful to the
members of the subcommittee for working on a bipartisan basis
to champion Tribal priorities. With a total request of $4.6
billion for Indian Affairs programs, this budget will address
complex and difficult challenges, such as the missing and
murdered indigenous people's crisis, the legacy and continuing
impacts of Federal Indian boarding school policies, and native
language revitalization.
Public safety continues to be a top priority for Tribal
leaders across the country. The budget includes $651 million to
support critical public safety needs across all of Indian
Country. We also request $1.5 billion for Indian education
programs, with strong investments in the day-to-day operations
of schools. This funding is critical as we prepare the next
generation of indigenous Americans to lead their communities.
Turning to wildland fire, we continue to see the
devastating impacts wildland fires are having across the
country. I want to thank the subcommittee for your bipartisan
support for extending supplemental fire pay for another year.
The 2025 budget invests in reforms, including $75 million, to
support permanent pay increases for Federal and Tribal wildland
firefighters.
Stewardship of our natural resources is a core mission for
us. Interior manages about 20 percent of America's lands and is
responsible for protection and recovery of more than 2,300
endangered and threatened species. Our request includes $2.8
billion in annual funding for conservation efforts that support
key initiatives, such as wildlife corridors and implementing
the National Seed Strategy. I am proud of the proposal of $8
million for a mandatory funded Tribal land acquisition program,
a top priority for Tribes, as part of our implementation of the
Land and Water Conservation Fund Program. This proposal honors
the role Tribes play as stewards of the land and will help
ensure they have resources to ensure healthy lands for future
generations.
The 2025 budget invests $189 million to continue the
progress we have made in deploying clean energy, building a
resilient domestic-based supply chain, and creating thousands
of good-paying jobs. The demand for renewable energy has never
been greater, and Interior is leading the way to a clean energy
future. Regarding infrastructure, our request includes $2.7
billion to fund operations and maintenance for our more than
130,000 buildings and structures and 65,000 miles of public
roads. In addition, there is $1.6 billion in mandatory funding
available in 2025 through the Great American Outdoors Act
Legacy Restoration Fund. We are currently executing 326 GAOA-
funded projects with 83 additional projects requested for 2025.
We cannot address our major maintenance needs through annual
appropriations alone. I look forward to working with Congress
to reauthorize the GAOA Legacy Restoration Fund.
Overall, the President's budget request for Interior
invests in programs to strengthen our Nation for all Americans.
This great work would not be possible without the dedication of
career public servants at Interior. I look forward to our
continued work together on these important issues. Thank you
for your partnership and support for the important work of the
Department and its incredible employees. I am pleased to answer
any questions you may have.
[The statement of Secretary Haaland follows:]
[GRAPHIC(S) NOT AVAILABLE IN TIFF FORMAT]

WIND ENERGY: LAVA RIDGE

Mr. Simpson. Thank you, Secretary. Let me start off. This
is something you knew was going to come up and I was going to
have to comment on. The proposal to build a 400 turbine wind-
generating facility in Idaho's Magic Valley has been met with
almost unanimous opposition from local residents. These would
be the largest wind turbines on land in the United States. They
are taller than the Washington monument, they are taller than
the Seattle Space Needle, 400 of them. I have yet to talk to
one Magic Valley resident who supports it. As the voice of my
constituents, I have been a very strong, vocal opponent of the
Lava Ridge Wind Project and have advocated for the Bureau of
Land Management to listen to Idaho's concerns.
To force the BLM to slow down and acknowledge the concerns
of impact communities, I included language in the 2024
appropriation legislation, stopping the project from moving
forward until BLM consults with local elected officials and
stakeholders on alternative actions to reduce the impact to
wildlife, cultural resources, and other assets. Can you walk me
through what the Agency has done since the enactment of the
2024 bill to adhere to the requirements of the Idaho wind
provision? What elected officials or stakeholders have you
consulted with since March 9, and what feedback and concerns
have they shared with you, and how do you plan to incorporate
those concerns before moving forward?
Secretary Haaland. Thank you for the question, Chairman,
and I also appreciate the conversation that we had yesterday on
this issue.
Roughly 26 government-to-government consultation meetings
with Tribes have happened. We have had at least 19 meetings
with county commissioners from Lincoln, Jerome, and Minidoka
counties, 14 briefings for Members of Congress or their staff,
six meetings with local organizations, four meetings with
grazing permittees and other members of the agricultural
community, six public meetings on the draft environmental
impact statement, numerous engagements with the Japanese-
American community, and many more. We appreciate your concern
and your communication with us, and we are working to conduct
additional consultations with local elected officials and
stakeholders. This issue is not completed yet. It is ongoing.
There are still more conversations and work to be had, and we
will obviously stay in touch with you as it moves forward.
Mr. Simpson. I appreciate that, and I appreciate the
consultation with the local community. This goes without
saying, but I expect BLM to listen to the concerns of Idahoans
directly affected by the proposed Lava Ridge Wind Project and
give those concerns considerable weight in the development of
the final EIS. Local officials, affected farmers, Tribes, and
the Japanese-American community have raised serious concerns,
and their voices need to be heard. I support an all-of-the-
above approach to energy development, but this administration
cannot continue to unilaterally ram through renewable energy
projects with no local support and with no forethought for
their impact on these important land uses. The Lava Ridge Wind
Project is out of touch with Idahoans, and I remain committed
to taking significant actions to put the people of Idaho first,
so thank you for that.

SAGE GROUSE

Sage grouse management plan. Since 2015, I have included
language in the annual Interior spending bills prohibiting the
greater sage grouse from being listed under the Endangered
Species Act, and Congress has directed BLM to work
collaboratively with States, private landowners, and other
stakeholders to conserve sage grouse habitat. In 2015, Idaho
adopted the collaboratively-developed Idaho Sage Grouse
Management Plan as the State's official policy for sage grouse
management. The original plan has been updated to provide a
balanced approach for the species and the people of Idaho.
Since 2015, the State of Idaho has spent over $5 million to
increase data on sage grouse populations and enhance over
76,000 acres of sage grouse habitat. These efforts aren't
unique to Idaho. Other States have worked just as tirelessly
and dedicated significant State resources to avoid the need for
Federal rulemaking.
Despite these efforts, on March 15, 2024, the Department
published proposals regarding greater sage grouse habitat. The
threat of an ESA listing and overly-restrictive Federal land
use plans undermines the collaborative work being done to
manage sage grouse habitats at the local level by the
individuals who work, live, and recreate on Idaho's land. More
so, the proposals will have drastic effects on grazing
permittees, resource development, timber harvesters, recreators
whose livelihoods depend on responsible management of our
Federal lands.
Question one. Let me tell you how we got here because
people think that I don't care about sage grouse and the fact
that I don't want them to go to extinct or anything else. When
this became an issue, former Secretary Salazar came to me and
said what we need to do is have the States develop management
plans. All of the States, 11 Western States, put together these
collaborative efforts that included Federal land management
agencies, local ranchers, recreationists, others, stakeholders,
and they came up with what they believed to be good State
management plans that they could live with, and Idaho's plan
was actually rated as co-equal with the BLM's plan.
We all know that you are not going to save sage grouse
habitat if you don't get private landowners involved because a
lot of the habitat is on private lands. They had an interest in
this, and they worked very hard with the State and the
committee to accomplish that. Now we come in, and the reason I
include language to not list them is that if all of a sudden
you listed them, those people that participated in developing a
sage grouse management plan are going to say, I am done, I am
out of here, and that is going to hurt trying to save sage
grouse habitat and stuff. That is my concern with listing, and
that is why I put the language in there that I have had.
Is the Department's position that a one-size-fits-all
approach to conservation outweighs unique plans that consider
and incorporate the specific threats to that locality, and can
you explain the Department's process leading to the necessity
for this proposal, and what is the timing and plan for this
proposal?
Secretary Haaland. Thank you very much, Chairman, and it is
been my experience with the Department that we very much value
local input, State input. We feel those folks know better. They
live on the land and they know things that we don't know, so we
are transparent and very collaborative when it comes to that.
With respect to the plan itself, we published a draft EIS for
the greater sage grouse plan in March. The comment period ends
June 13. We aim to complete revised plan amendments by the end
of 2024. We are working with partners. BLM identified specific
greater sage grouse plan decisions to consider amending to
incorporate new science. All other existing decisions remain in
the plans unchanged, and DOI remains committed to reversing the
downward trend in greater sage grouse populations on BLM
managed lands.
Mr. Simpson. Let me suggest that when this comes out, you
need to incorporate not just the comments and stuff and listen
to the comments from, from people, but get together a working
group and say, okay, how are we going to implement that if that
is the way we are moving forward on this, and what are the
impacts going to be, and what are the impacts on onshore energy
production and mineral development because one of the most
critical problems we are facing in this country right now is
the supply chain for critical minerals that we get from our
adversaries, frankly. We have those resources in this country.
Most of them are on public lands, though. Trying to get access
to those resources is sometimes very, very difficult. All of
those things need to be taken into consideration if you are
going to change the sage grouse plan.
As I said, my biggest concern is that it involves local
people because you are not going to save sage grouse habitat if
you don't have private landowners involved in it.
Secretary Haaland. Thank you.
Mr. Simpson. Ms. Pingree.

CLIMATE RESILIENCE FUNDING

Ms. Pingree. Thanks, Mr. Chair. I brought this up in my
opening remarks, but I want to just talk a little bit about
Tribal climate resilience funding. I guess we know Tribal
climate resilience needs are as diverse as climate impacts seem
to affect each Tribe differently. I know a little bit about the
struggles of the Passamaquoddy Indian Tribe in Maine who are
confronting climate-related environmental threats, such as sea
level rise, which imperils important cultural sites, Tribal
member housing, including an elderly housing facility, as well
as wastewater treatment plant. The Bipartisan Infrastructure
Law, Inflation Reduction Act, and annual Tribal climate
resilience appropriated funds provide about $120 million a year
for Tribal climate adaptation and resilience projects. Can you
tell us sort of the nationwide need for these funds,
specifically in the lower 48, and how many Tribes are applying
right now?
Secretary Haaland. Thank you very much for the question,
and, yes, that is definitely an issue and we are paying
attention to it. We have had, of course, a very strong response
from Indian Country. We have received 166 proposals from Tribes
totaling over $150 million for the fiscal year 2023 funded
solicitation, and recently announced $120 million in awards for
146 projects for 102 Tribes and nine Tribal organizations. It
is historically high funding with the help of the Bipartisan
Infrastructure Law and the Inflation Reduction Act.
Ms. Pingree. Are you working with some of the other Federal
agencies to try to leverage more funds or see how we can
enhance the amount of money available?
Secretary Haaland. Pretty much everything we do with
respect to this issue and Tribes is an all-of-government
approach. That is the direction from President Biden, so, yes,
we are absolutely working together to make sure that we are
doing this very concerted effort.
Ms. Pingree. Great. Do you have an estimate of the universe
of need for funds to address the impacts of climate change on
Tribes, and how are you guys strategizing about funding once
the Infrastructure Law and the Inflation Reduction Act funding
runs out, which changed things a lot.
Secretary Haaland. Yes, of course, and as time goes on,
things seemingly get somewhat worse with sea level rise and the
like, but we are going to keep an eye on it and work to fill
the need as we see fit, but certainly that is a priority for
us, and it is a priority for Tribes. One of the things that I
feel very confident about is that we are making sure that
Tribes are leading their efforts themselves. We don't want to
tell them what to do. They will tell us what to do. With
respect to the other Federal agencies, it is FEMA and CEQ who
will help us identify other resources. The 2025 budget also
includes $49 million with a $6 million increase for Tribal
adaptation and relocation planning grants.
Ms. Pingree. Great. Well, thank you for that. I know we are
all anxious to work with you going forward because it seems
like, just what I have ascertained from one Tribe in my state,
that the need is significant, and as I said, we weren't even
talking about Alaska when we talk about that, and that is just
another big pool. Ask one more?
Mr. Simpson. Yeah.
Ms. Pingree. Yeah. Just one more other question right now.
Last week, Acadia National Park announced that the park will be
receiving $950,000 in funding from the Inflation Reduction Act
to create a model for addressing climate change vulnerabilities
of coastal archaeological sites, collections, landscapes, and
ethnographic resources, something we have a lot of in Maine,
including in my own community. Acadia will be using the two-
eyed seeing model to meaningfully integrate indigenous
knowledge with Western science. The project will build on 15
years of consultation at Acadia National Park. St. Croix Island
International Historic Site, and Roosevelt Campobello
International Park with the Wabanaki Tribes. This is just one
example of the amazing work the Inflation Reduction funding
will support to prepare parks across the country to be
resilient to climate change. The National Park Service just
announced projects to be funded by the full $195 million. I
know a little bit about what is going on in my own State, but I
would like to know what other types of work going on and how
the funding will work to support the work across the park
system.
Secretary Haaland. Yes. Thank you so much. We have been
extremely proud of the work we have been able to do with
Tribes. I appreciate you mentioning Indigenous knowledge. We
believe very strongly that the science is what we all need to
pay attention to, and with Indigenous knowledge they are really
helping us to steward these lands.
We are implementing Tribal co-stewardship agreements across
the country. We have signed probably 200 of those, and there
are probably 60 more that we are working on to ensure that we
are not missing anything because Tribes have the experience.
Yes, more than one-third of coastal parks are at significant
risk from sea level rise, coastal erosion, and storm surge that
is happening on all of our coasts. The NPS budget includes $4
million for additional climate vulnerability studies and $2.5
million for climate-related natural resource projects at the
parks. Both of those investments will help the National Park
Service make progress in addressing climate resiliency. We will
keep our eyes on that and continue to work as needed, and will
certainly bring Tribes into the fold when it comes to how we
steward those lands.
Ms. Pingree. Great. Thank you so much for that work. I will
yield back, Mr. Chair.

LEAD AMMUNITION

Mr. Edwards. Yeah, coming in loud and clear. Thank you,
Madam Secretary. I appreciate that you are with us here today.
Under your watch, on September 16, 2022, the U.S. Fish and
Wildlife Service issued a rule that included a ban on
traditional ammunition and later took additional steps to
further ban traditional ammunition. What scientific evidence
was used to support the 2022 final rule to put a ban on
traditional ammunition and subsequent actions?
Secretary Haaland. Thank you very much, Congressman, for
the question. I can start out by saying that the Fish and
Wildlife Service is taking a measured approach to evaluate the
use of lead on refuge areas. They committed in the 2022-2023
Hunt-Fish Rule to consider the future of lead use based on the
large number of public comments received on the issue. It is
our job to steward our national wildlife refuges, and we are
just making sure that we are doing that in a responsible way.
Mr. Edwards. No scientific research is what I think I heard
your answer to be.
Secretary Haaland. Yes, we have scientists all over the
country studying these things, Congressman, and I believe that
is what----
Mr. Edwards. Is there a specific report that you can point
this committee to that caused you to take that action?
Secretary Haaland. I would be very happy to get back with
the Fish and Wildlife Service and make sure that they can give
you the data that they used in evaluating this issue.
Mr. Edwards. I would be very interested to hear that from
an organization that is all about science and studies. Can we
count on your word to provide that data to this committee?
Secretary Haaland. We will be happy to get in touch with
your staff as soon as this hearing is over, sir.
Mr. Edwards. All right. Thank you.
Mr. Simpson. The committee will follow up on that.
Mr. Edwards. All right. I appreciate it.

ADHERENCE TO FEDERAL LAWS

Mr. Edwards. Next question. Secretary Haaland, just yes or
no, do you feel that it is prudent to provide funding to
localities that intentionally violate Federal law?
Secretary Haaland. I don't understand what the question is.
Mr. Edwards. Real simple. Should we provide funding to
entities or localities that intentionally violate Federal law?
Secretary Haaland. Could you be more specific, sir? I don't
know----
Mr. Edwards. Yeah. sure. Do you feel like it is prudent to
provide funding to localities that intentionally violate
Federal law, any Federal law?
Secretary Haaland. I couldn't answer that question unless I
knew the specific issue.
Mr. Edwards. I mean, there is a whole host of Federal laws.
Secretary Haaland. But----
Mr. Edwards. Would you not expect local----
Secretary Haaland. But of course, we follow the law,
Congressman. The Department of the Interior follows the law in
everything we do.
Mr. Edwards. Would you expect that localities would also
follow Federal law?
Secretary Haaland. I expect every American to follow the
laws.
Mr. Edwards. All right. Thank you for that. With that, do
you feel the Department of Interior should continue to provide
grant funds to Tribes that legalize marijuana against Federal
law?
Secretary Haaland. I suppose it depends, Congressman, on
what the grant is used for. If it is a grant to help them
combat sea level rise and move their communities----
Mr. Edwards. My question was in regard----
Secretary Haaland [continuing]. That is one issue.
Mr. Edwards. My question was in regards to legalizing
marijuana on Tribal lands. Do you think that we should continue
to fund Tribal entities that break Federal law by legalizing
marijuana?
Secretary Haaland. I would imagine if Tribes are requesting
funds and they fill out the applicable paperwork and make their
grant proposals, that they will be reviewed and decided upon
in----
Mr. Edwards. What other Federal laws would you be in
support of local entities breaking?
Secretary Haaland. Congressman, I am not in support of
anyone breaking any laws.
Mr. Edwards. Okay. I think by virtue of that answer, you
would not condone continuing to fund local entities that break
Federal law.
Secretary Haaland. I would also make the point,
Congressman, that the Justice Department is the Department to
handle any issues of lawbreaking, and we are happy to point you
in that direction, if you would like us to.
Mr. Edwards. I would just like to interject and let you
know my opinion on the topic. There have been several studies
from the National Institute on Drug Abuse that have linked
heavy marijuana use to lower income, greater welfare
dependence, unemployment, criminal behavior, and lower life
satisfaction. As our Nation continues to face an unprecedented
drug crisis and mental health crisis, I think it is more
imperative than ever that Federal agencies commit to upholding
Federal law and protecting our communities against the hazards
that are caused by marijuana production and sale.

TRIBAL CLIMATE RESILIENCE

Mr. Kilmer. Thank you, Chairman, and thank you, Madam
Secretary, for being here, and I appreciate the ranking member

The President's budget includes a little south of $49
million for the Tribal Climate Resilience Program, including an
increase of $4 million for climate relocation grants. That
really matters in my neck of the woods, Madam Secretary. You
came and visited my district. If any of the members of the
committee want to Google ``Taholah,'' which is the lower
village of the Quinault Indian Nation, and flooding, you will
actually see photographs of people canoeing through the streets
of their village. I sat there with the former president of the
Quinault Indian Nation, and she said when I was a kid, the
ocean was a football field slant away, and she said, now it is
our front porch, and every time there is a severe storm, there
are seawall breaches, and they just fill up like a bowl.
Unfortunately, that is the case for a whole bunch of the
Tribes in my neck of the woods that are trying to move to
higher ground, and some of this costs money. For the Quinault
Relocation Project alone, we are talking a $400 million project
to move a village out of harm's way, but funding isn't the only
challenge. We also have this sort of complex jumble of
potential resources and programs across the Federal Government,
each with its own set of rules, and regulatory streams, and
cost shares, and deadlines, and other administrative processes
that kind of make it difficult and sometimes impossible for
Tribes to plan and execute a project.
I really appreciate the chairman. He has partnered with me
on a bill called the TERRA Act that would establish an
interagency framework under your Department, Madam Secretary,
for just coordinating the prevention and mitigation and
relocation efforts of Tribes. The idea under this bill would be
to work directly with Department of Interior to develop a
single comprehensive plan that would integrate and streamline
some of these Federal resources and programs across the Federal
Government. I think that really matters because time is of the
essence. These conflicting rules and deadlines and things like
that are not helpful to the folks who just need some help.
Would love to just get your thoughts on how that
streamlined authority that exists under the proposal that we
have introduced could help your Agency and could help Tribes
actually develop and execute a comprehensive and coordinated
plan for climate-driven relocation. I want to just invite if
there are other resources or approaches that you are pursuing
toward that end.
Secretary Haaland. Thank you so much for the question, and,
yes, we talked about this the other day. In fact, it sounds
like a very exciting idea, and, of course, if we can coordinate
and streamline things, that is a win for Tribes in this case,
so we appreciate that, as well as community-driven projects.
You know, when we use the word ``relocation,'' it is a
buzzword that gives me a little bad feeling inside when we
think about the other relocation for Tribes from years past.
These ideas for Tribes to relocate come from within their
communities, and so we want to be as supportive as we possibly
can. Of course, please feel free to reach out to us. Your staff
is welcome to call our staff, have a conversation about the
bill to find out details, and we are more than happy to work
with you as we move forward. I think when folks get together
and talk about things, they bring ideas to the table that they
didn't realize until they are there. We would love to have more
conversations about it with you.

CLIMATE CHANGE

Lastly, a discussion, secondly, over climate change. You
have pointed out that you use science, and when I was
Secretary, I had the honor of making sure my director of the
USGS was an astronaut and had a Ph.D. in geology. You talk
about sea level change in science. Have you ever read the
multi-agency Department of Interior Climate Change report,
dated 2017?
Secretary Haaland. No, I haven't.
Mr. Zinke. Madam Secretary, I would suggest you read that--
--
Secretary Haaland. Thank you, Congressman.
Mr. Zinke [continuing]. It came from your Department. In
that, have you ever heard the term, ``glacial isostatic
adjustment?''
Secretary Haaland. I have not.
Mr. Zinke. Okay. Glacial isostatic adjustment on the East
Coast is because we had glaciers, large, thick, heavy glaciers,
on the plate to the north in Maine all the way down to Jersey.
When that plate moves and you have the glaciers on it and the
glaciers are removed over time, there is an isostatic
adjustment. If you had read that report, it would have talked
about it, and it also would have talked about sea level rise
versus depression. I know you are probably not aware, but the
sea level rise fully compensated for, also the shift, the
sinking, is about 1 millimeter a year. That would be in the
report. Mr. Chairman, I yield back.

GULF OF MEXICO: OIL AND GAS; RICE'S WHALE

Mr. Simpson. Thank you. Mr. Ellzey.

Secretary, for being here. I will get right to the questions.
Over the last several decades, the Gulf of Mexico has been
a multi-use basin. However, the Bureau of Ocean Energy
Management recently published its notice of availability of the
Area Identification, or Area ID, for the Proposed Gulf of
Mexico Lease Sales 262, 263, and 264, and is already

Mr. Ellzey. Okay. Well, with the rice's whale critical

Secretary Haaland. I appreciate that.

Secretary Haaland. Congressman, I apologize. We are happy
to get back with your office as soon as----
Mr. Ellzey. Okay. That is fine.
Secretary Haaland [continuing]. I have a moment to answer
that specific question.
Mr. Ellzey. Sure. Sure. You only have 8 billion topics that

Secretary Haaland. Thank you.
Mr. Ellzey [continuing]. I don't expect you to have a

you.
Secretary Haaland. Absolutely. We will be happy to comment.
[The information follows:]

The Department strongly supports safe and responsible domestic
energy production that relies on the best available science.
On April 1, 2024, BOEM published the Notice of Availability of the
Area Identification (Area ID) for proposed Gulf of Mexico (GOM) Oil and
Gas Lease Sales 262, 263, and 264. The Area ID includes all available
acreage in the Gulf of Mexico Program Area (approximately 94.1 million
acres) for consideration of leasing and environmental analysis. This
includes areas that have been proposed by the National Marine Fisheries
Service as critical habitat for the critically endangered Rice's Whale.
No exclusions have been implemented at this time.
Please note that the Area ID is not a decision to lease and is not
a prejudgment by the Department of the Interior on how or whether to
proceed with proposed Lease Sales 262, 263, and 264 under the National
OCS Oil and Gas Leasing Program. The Area ID simply identifies which
areas warrant further consideration and analyses as BOEM's leasing
process unfolds.
For more information about the 2024-2029 National OCS Program Area
ID, visit BOEM's website, https://www.boem.gov/oil-gas-energy/national-
program/2024-2029-gom-area-identification.

currently.

ENDANGERED SPECIES ACT RULE

Mr. Simpson. Sounded like a ``yes'' to me. Madam Secretary,
last month, the administration released a decision reversing
the critical Endangered Species Act reforms implemented during
the previous administration, reinstating the blanket 4(d) Rule,
which automatically provides for endangered level protections
to species listed only as threatened. I have heard concerns
that this change will lead to less flexibility for landowners
and stakeholders and could tie up landowners in mountains of
unnecessary bureaucratic red tape, ceasing any economic
progress for rural communities across the United States. Can
you walk us through what these changes are and how they will
improve the Endangered Species Act, and what resources has the
Department provided to private landowners to understand this
rulemaking, and did the Department consider the negative
impacts related to economic development associated with this
rulemaking?
Secretary Haaland. Thank you, Congressman. The Fish and
Wildlife Service finalized three rules to restore protections
for species in their habitats, strengthen processes for
listing, designating critical habitats in consultation with
Federal partners. The changes do not further impede on private
landowners rights. Rules are not expected to change the number
or frequency of listings and de-listings, the total amount of
area of critical habitat designated, the rate or frequency of
Section 7 consultation, or the resulting conclusion.
The rules do provide clear, straightforward regulations to
recover listed species and protect critical habitats for
Section 7 seven consultations, species classification, and
critical habitat. There is a separate rule finalized to
encourage participation in voluntary, collaborative
conservation with resource managers and landowners. I know that
we are always in contact with stakeholders whenever any of
these issues come to light, so I want to assure you that we
operate in a transparent manner and certainly do welcome those
comments.
Mr. Simpson. I appreciate that, but there is a difference
in being in contact with local stakeholders and listening to
local stakeholders.
Secretary Haaland. We listen.
Mr. Simpson. There's a difference----
Secretary Haaland. We do listen.
Mr. Simpson [continuing]. They ought to have some input in
this, but it sounds to me like it is not going to change
anything. If it is not going to change anything, then why the
hell are we doing it?
Secretary Haaland. Thank you, Congressman. We are doing it
because I think it will make things better.
Mr. Simpson. Okay. Well, we will have----
Secretary Haaland. It will clarify certain things.

CALDWELL CANYON MINE

phosphate reserves, and these reserves have been in production
for many years. One such mine, the Caldwell Canyon Mine, is

mine is of great importance not only to my constituents and our
State's economy, but also to the country's agricultural
industry. When complete, Caldwell Canyon Mine will provide the
only source of phosphate in the United States, which is
critically important to our Nation and the world's food supply.

later this year. This schedule could result in a final EIS and
ROD in early 2025. Does that answer your question, sir?

TRIBAL LAW ENFORCEMENT

CLIMATE CHANGE AND SEA LEVEL RISE

Ms. Pingree. Thank you very much, Mr. Chair, and thank you,

I just want to tell you, this year, we had two back-to-back

fishing town 12 miles off the coast of Maine on an island,
thought there would ever be anything like we saw this year. Two
back-to-back storms. We have 180-year-old boatyard, basically,
in our district. In our little town of 400, everybody turned
out between the first and the second storm because they were so
worried that this building that has been there forever and the
fishermen depend on was going to wash away. They had block and
tackles and chains. They put cement blocks on the floor. They
did everything they could possibly think of to try to hold it
on land, to try to keep it there. Right now, we don't know if
it will survive. It got through that storm, it got through the
second storm, but we are probably going to lose it.
You can come visit the coast of Maine and see up and down
the coast of Maine, 150-year-old piers everywhere. Communities
like Stonington, Maine, fishing towns, they are washing away.
This was a tide like we had never seen before and storm surge
from the southeast, which is weather we used to never have
before. You can say that there is some kind of tectonic plate
shift going on or this isn't really real or whatever, but I
invite you to come to my communities. These are fishermen.
These are communities that are going to disappear, not to
mention the Tribal communities. I am just talking about our
fishing towns, our community piers. They are going away. This
tide is higher than we have ever imagined could happen to us,
and it is not even there yet. It is not even anywhere near what
the maps say, places that our peninsulas are going to turn into
islands. Communities are losing their sewer systems, their
infrastructure. It is all going away.
It is one thing to sit inland. In Montana, you got
mountains, you can run to the top, but we don't have those
options. However we want to describe why this is happening, I
just want to thank you, Madam Secretary, and your Department,
for putting this focus on because this is extremely real to us.
It is very emotional to us. It is the end of so many of our
communities, and we don't know where we are going to get the
money to deal with all this. We don't know how we are going to
move to higher ground when there isn't higher ground or it is
already filled up. Our island communities, our coastal
