your attention to us. I thoroughly invite anyone from this

Republican conservatives who don't always vote for me, but they
want to know what we are going to do about this, and I invite
you to join us in doing that.
Secretary Haaland. Ranking Member, if I could just say very

Ms. Pingree. Thank you for that.

Tuesday, April 30, 2024.

FISCAL YEAR 2025 REQUEST FOR THE ENVIRONMENTAL PROTECTION AGENCY

WITNESSES

HON. MICHAEL S. REGAN, ADMINISTRATOR, ENVIRONMENTAL PROTECTION AGENCY
FAISAL AMIN, CHIEF FINANCIAL OFFICER, ENVIRONMENTAL PROTECTION AGENCY
Mr. Simpson. The hearing will come to order. We are here to
discuss the President's budget proposal for the EPA for fiscal
year 2025. I care about protecting and preserving our land and
water resources to guarantee their long-term sustainability,
but there also needs to be an appropriate balance between
protecting our environment and promoting economic growth.
Unfortunately, I fear that this EPA, with its rush to regulate,
is making it harder for investors to thrive here at home and
threatening the reliability of the electrical grid. Just last
week, EPA finalized four power plant rules that severely
threaten affordable, reliable power in this country.
When I am home in Idaho, I often hear from farmers,
ranchers, small businesses, and many others who are concerned
about their very existence given the growing cost to comply
with EPA regulations. Of particular interest and relevance to
my district is the Waters of the United States rule, which we
have discussed with Mr. Regan in the past. The Supreme Court
ruled that it was too broad. I will cover this and other EPA
regulations during my questions, but I will just point out the
clear overreach of the administration on some of these
regulations.
The President's fiscal year 2025 budget requests totals
just under $11 billion. The Fiscal Responsibility Act forced
the Agency to reduce its budget request by $1 billion compared
to last year's request, but I am still astounded by the
proposed increase of over 2,000 FTEs, which is more FTEs than
what was proposed in last year's budget request. This is a
glaring sign of the Agency's priorities. Compared to last
year's request when the Agency was forced to request less, it
chooses to take hits to programs that go directly to our States
and Tribes. Even still, the fiscal year 2025 request of $10.99
billion is $1.8 billion or 20 percent above the enacted level.
If enacted, this would be the highest level of funding for the
EPA in history. Not included in this request is the more than
$100 billion EPA received from several large spending packages
outside of the annual appropriations process. Already the
Agency has hired an additional 1,200 FTEs using this
supplemental funding, but this money is temporary, not
permanent, and I am concerned that the Agency is going to be
pinning Congress with a hiring cliff down the road.
Lastly, it is concerning that the budget proposes to reduce
or eliminate many bipartisan popular programs and grants that
go directly to States, Tribes, and local governments. I know
many of my colleagues have questions for you today. We are
ready to have tough conversations about how best to spend our
limited Federal resources to ensure our natural resources are
protected and preserved while still promoting economic
development and job creation. Thank you, Administrator, for
being here today to have this discussion with us. Now, I would
like to yield to my ranking member, Ms. Pingree, for her
opening statement.
Ms. Pingree. Thank you so much, Mr. Chair. Welcome back to
the subcommittee, Mr. Regan and Mr. Amin. Thank you both for
being here with us today.
The EPA budget request totals $11 billion, an increase of
1.8 billion over the enacted level, to support its mission to
protect human health and the environment. The request proposes
substantial increases to address the climate crisis through
robust funding for the EPA science and technology and
environmental programs. It makes important investments in
environmental justice so that we can continue to address
historic underinvestment in underserved and overburdened
communities. The budget request also makes significant
investments in the EPA's workforce, including funding for 2,000
new staff. Not only has the need for EPA's expertise expanded
tremendously over the last decade, but the growing challenges
around climate change require more expertise and more staff.
I want to acknowledge some of the important work the EPA
has done over the last year. You have established for the first
time a national and legally enforceable drinking water standard
for PFAS. This is a very big deal, and it will go a long way in
keeping families safe from these harmful chemicals. The EPA so
far has invested $9 billion to address PFAS, and it has issued
guidance on how to dispose of and destroy it, but there is more
work to be done, particularly when it comes to PFAS in
agricultural settings, which is a big issue for our farms in
Maine and I know in farms across the country. I hope we can
talk more about those efforts today.
The EPA has also made significant progress in environmental
justice, awarding millions in grant funding to communities all
around the country. This will help communities from Alaska to
Maine to lower energy costs, improve drinking water, breathe
cleaner air, and become more resilient during times of extreme
weather. I also appreciate how you are continuing to hold
polluters accountable. These efforts reduce our community's
exposure to hazardous waste and toxic chemicals.
Thank you so much for appearing before us today. We
appreciate your testimony, answers to our questions, and with
that, I yield back. Thank you, Mr. Chair.
Mr. Simpson. Thank you, Ranking Member Pingree. With that,
Administrator Regan, you may proceed with your opening
statement. Your official submitted testimony will be included
in the record.
Mr. Regan. Well, thank you, and, Chair Simpson, Ranking
Member Pingree, and members of the committee, thank you all for
the opportunity to appear before you to discuss the bold vision
laid out in the U.S. EPA's proposed fiscal year 2025 budget
request. Our partnership and open dialogue with Congress is
invaluable for EPA to carry out its mission to protect public
health and the environment.
Over the last year, we have been hard at work at EPA. Under
President Biden's leadership, my Agency has finalized
protections that will bring 100 million people cleaner and
safer drinking water free from PFAS, and we have worked hard to
right many of the historic wrongs communities have faced for
generations. Through our critical rulemaking, we banned the
last remaining kind of asbestos use in our country and issued
final technology-based standards that will eliminate more than
6,000 tons of toxic air pollution from chemical plants each
year, slashing cancer-causing pollution from covered processes
and equipment by 80 percent, and reducing elevated cancer risk
for those living near these facilities by an astounding 96
percent.
EPA is committed to protecting public health and the
environment for the American people, but more than just
powerful health impacts we are undertaking, my Agency is
working hard to implement the historic laws that you have
passed in President Biden's Investing in America agenda.
President Biden's Investing in America agenda has not only
directly invested in communities nationwide, but it has
generated nearly $700 billion in funding for private sector
manufacturing and clean energy projects, creating good paying
jobs and enhancing our global competitiveness. Together,
President Biden's Investing in America agenda and EPA's 2025
budget requests will continue to invest in environmental
actions that will promote cleaner communities and produce
economic benefits for years to come.
President Biden's proposed fiscal year 2025 budget request
for EPA provides nearly $11 billion to advance key priorities
for the American people, including protecting air quality,
cleaning up pollution, upgrading the Nation's aging water
infrastructure, urgently fighting the climate crisis, and
advancing environmental justice. Millions of people across the
country are still grappling with the effects of poor air
quality, perpetuating harmful health and economic impacts. In
fiscal year 2025, EPA will improve air quality for communities
by reducing emissions of ozone-forming pollutants, particulate
matter, and air toxics.
The President's budget includes $1.3 billion to improve air
quality for communities across the country to reduce exposure
to dangerous levels of radiation and to leverage regulatory
tools and public and private sector partnerships that promote
environmental stewardship and encourage the adoption of cost-
effective technologies and practices. EPA's work to set these
standards provides certainty to industry, builds on
advancements in technology, and reinforces market movement that
reduces power plant emissions without sacrificing reliable and
affordable energy.
Clean and safe water is the foundation for healthy
communities and a thriving economy. Although substantial
progress has been made, many areas across our Nation still face
significant barriers and challenges to achieving this goal.
Aging water infrastructure, the effects of lead pipes,
cybersecurity threats to our water system, climate change, and
emerging contaminants, such as PFAS, all pose dangerous health
risks to our Nation's water supply and to the American people.
EPA's budget request includes a total of $101 million for two
EPA grant programs dedicated to remediating lead contamination
in drinking water. Back in February, I joined Vice President
Harris in Pittsburgh, Pennsylvania to announce that EPA would
invest over $5 billion in drinking and clean water
infrastructure upgrades nationwide. This investment builds on
the historic funding for lead pipe replacement through the bill
and underscores the President's commitment to ensuring access
to safe drinking water and creating good-paying jobs in the
process.
From investing in clean air to cleaning up contaminated
land and water, there is no shortage of important work to be
done, but, members of this committee, I assure you EPA is up
for the task. We are eager to work with all of you to deliver
for our fellow Americans and to secure our Nation's global
competitiveness, but we need your support. The fiscal year 2025
President's budget continues the historic progress and
investments made by the Biden-Harris administration and
positions EPA to advance our vital mission of protecting public
health and the environment, while championing environmental
justice and tackling the climate crisis.
Thank you all for the opportunity to be here today to
submit testimony for the record, and I look forward to our
continued partnership to achieve these ambitious yet necessary
goals. Thank you.
[The statement of Mr. Regan follows:]

Mr. Simpson. Thank you, Administrator, for that opening
statement. I will start off by saying I was pleased that the
Supreme Court reined in the EPA with the Sackett decisions on
the Waters of the United States rule, a rule that has been
changing, and no one seems to be able to come up with a final
rule that we are going to be using. The decision narrowed what
waters are federally regulated under the Clean Water Act. I
have long been concerned that the Biden administration's
definition of ``Federal waters'' are overly broad and took
control away from the States. In fact, a lot of people don't
understand it. If the EPA doesn't control the waters, the
States regulate the waters. It is not that they are
unregulated. It is that the States regulate them, and the
overbroad rule that was written by the EPA takes that power
away from the States in many cases.
I was also concerned of the impact of the rule on farmers,
business developers, and even property owners, and I am glad
that the Supreme Court provided needed clarity on this issue,
but now the Agency needs to do its part to appropriately
implement the Court's decision. There are 27 States, including
Idaho, under the injunction. The remaining States are operating
under the Waters of the United States conforming rule. In both
cases, the Agency says it will implement the definition of
``WOTUS'' consistent with Sackett, but I have heard a lot of
questions and concerns about implementation, and I know you
have, too, which, by the way, I hope you will commit to
substantially responding to the letters you have received from
the regulated community.
How many requests have been submitted to the Agency for a
jurisdictional determination since the conforming rule was
finalized in September, and how many of those have been
approved? I understand that many of the requests are being
elevated to the headquarters for a decision. What percentage of
the requests are being elevated, and how long are those reviews
taking? Finally, what guidance is the EPA staff using to make
jurisdictional determinations under both the pre-2015 framework
and the conforming rule? Aside from what is already public on
your website, are there other guidance documents or memos that
have been issued or that you are planning to issue, and can you
make those documents public so that the regulated parties can
understand how you are implementing WOTUS?
Mr. Regan. Well, thank you for that question, Mr. Chairman,
and, yes, this has been an issue that has ping ponged for a
number of years, decades. I will say this, that I committed to
following the science and following the law when I took this
role, and when the Sackett the decision came from the Supreme
Court, that is no different. We are following the law. The
Sackett decision was very proscribed, and so we issued guidance
quickly after issuing our revised rule last fall and continue
to work with the Army Corps to be sure that we are following
the implementation desires of the Supreme Court.
What I will say is, with the finalization of that rule in
2023, the Army Corps of Engineers has resumed issuing
jurisdictional determinations that were previously paused in
light of the Sackett decision. We have codified the
clarifications, codified and clarified exclusions in support of
farmers, like prior converted cropland, and for ranches, like
the artificial ponds used for drinking water. There were a lot
of good things that were preserved as well.
What I can do is circle back with you on the specific
number of requests that we have received and what those
percentages look like. I can tell you that there is agreement,
whether it is in our regional offices or headquarters, that we
are following the proscribed direction of the Supreme Court and
want to ensure that we provide certainty to our States as soon
as possible.
Mr. Simpson. Thank you, and I would appreciate circling
back on that with us.
Mr. Regan. Absolutely.

thank you, Administrator, for being here today. I am going to
focus my comments on an issue that we have talked about before,
which is air quality in the Central Valley and especially air
quality monitoring.
The American Lung Association released their 2024 State of
the Air report, and they found that last year was the worst
year for air quality in 25 years for the number of days with
hazardous or very unhealthy air quality. As you know, my
district is ranked one of the most polluted in the entire
country. This is a major issue. I had childhood asthma as a
kid. My brother had it. I don't want my two daughters to grow
up with the same challenges that I had. One source of this air
pollution is catastrophic wildfires, and as you know, Congress
put together a Bipartisan Wildfire Mitigation and Management
Commission that came out with a report last year that
highlighted some issues of what we can do to deal with air
quality and specifically air quality challenges coming from
these wildfires.
One of the Commission's recommendations was to improve the
EPA's air quality monitoring so people had real-time alerts,
that there was a nationally consistent smoke monitoring system
that could convey real information on poor air quality to the
public. I am working on bipartisan legislation right now to
expand those recommendations into law, but I would love to hear
from you about what you think we could do to help give
resources to the EPA to improve AirNow's capacity to be a real
state-of-the-art resource for the public during smoke events?
Mr. Regan. Well, thank you for that question, and, yes, I
think that the Commission's report has been very instructive to
us. We do have a very complicated air monitoring system across
the country. We do have the AirNow system, which, you know, is
on mobile apps or laptops where parents can take a look at that
and see what the air quality is to know if they want to send
their kid to a soccer game or go out jogging and such, and so
we feel really good about a robust air quality monitoring
system. One of the things that we have requested in this budget
that seems to be continually ignored or getting struck down or
saying EPA is trying to grow itself is developing the
technological capabilities, right? We know we are going to have
wildfires. We know that our forestry system needs to use
prescribed burns, so we have to do a better job of
understanding where the air pollution as a result is coming
from and how we protect public health.
This Commission that you refer to has been extremely
helpful, and you will see in our budget a resource request
specifically to enhance our air quality monitoring capabilities
and our ability to share that information nationwide. It used
to be that these wildfires were just on the West Coast. Now we
are seeing them in North Carolina. We are seeing wildfires come
from Canada. We need those investments, both technology and
people, to keep the American public safe.
Mr. Harder. Thank you. While I look forward to advocating
for those resources to go to the EPA, I think it is absolutely
imperative that we upgrade AirNow's capacity to be a real-time
resource. I think it is great what the mobile app does today,
but as the Commission highlighted, it is not enough.
Mr. Regan. Yeah.
Mr. Harder. There are opportunities to improve. I think EPA
can be a real leader towards getting that real-time information
directly to the public so folks can make informed decisions
about when they go outside where they go and making sure they
are keeping their kids safe.
The second question that I wanted to focus on is, in my
district, we have a national laboratory experimental test site
known as Site 300. This was created many, many decades ago when
it was all just a bunch of farmland around. Now there has been
major residential development over the last few decades. In
fact, my own home is just a few miles away from this
experimental test site. To protect some of the workers and
nearby residents, EPA came out and commented on the draft
environmental impact study for Lawrence Livermore Lab to add
air quality monitoring facilities so folks had a good sense of
some of the radiological constituents and criteria pollutants.
Can you walk me through the timeline for implementing those air
monitors? What is actually necessary to make sure that they are
procured and installed so cities like Mountain House and Tracy
have real information about what is going on in this
experimental test site?
Mr. Regan. Well, thank you for that question, and, you
know, there are air monitors currently at that site. I do want
to be clear that EPA is providing oversight over the Department
of Energy's long-term investment and cleanup of the site, so
they are in the driver's seat. We are in that advisory
capacity. The timeline question and those next steps, I would
say that DOE is in a better position to answer that question.
Mr. Harder. We will follow up with them, but you did
recommend or the EPA Region 9 did recommend additional air
quality monitoring facilities for the EIS that came out pretty
recently. Do you have any sense of when that would happen, or
that is a DOE----
Mr. Regan. Yeah. I know that Secretary Granholm and I have
a great working relationship. They have been very receptive to
our recommendations. I am quite certain that we are, as I
understand it, working in full partnership. Again, in terms of
the total receipt of those recommendations from one agency to
the other and the timing for the implementation of those
recommendations, I would have to leave that to DOE. I would not
want to get in front of them on that.
Mr. Harder. Okay. Well, I agreed with the EPA
recommendations. I think additional air quality monitoring
makes perfect sense in that site and will look forward to
working with you to make that happen. Thank you, and I yield
back.
Mr. Regan. Thank you.

Mr. Regan [continuing]. That specific number because some

Mr. Regan. Well, there are co-benefits there. Someone who

NO
X
reductions, but we also may get some
CO
2
reductions based on the technology that the

Mr. Regan. Sure.

Mr. Regan. No, no, not at all. I think what we are doing is

just announced, there were four rules that focused on carbon,
mercury, water, and coal ash cleanup. When I spent time with
the utility industry 2 years ago, they said, hey, instead of
dying by a thousand paper cuts, just present these regulations
in bundles so that we can make longer term investments because

simultaneous manner so that we know what we are getting and

additional FTEs.

Mr. Edwards. All right. Let's shift gears just a little
bit. Less than a week ago, on Thursday, April 26th, your Agency
issued a new regulation that expedites the requirement for
coal-fired power plants to reduce their greenhouse gas
emissions by 90 percent by the year 2039. What is your
instruction to them? What is the plan? What actions will they
take do you think to reduce their emissions by 90 percent?
Mr. Regan. You know, it will vary. I think what Duke Energy
will do in North Carolina will be drastically different than
what happens in West Virginia or Texas. There is a process that
we have in place. They have a number of years to comply. As a
former regulator, I will tell you each State secretary will
work with their utility to design a State implementation plan.
Mr. Edwards. What are the possibilities?
Mr. Regan. There are a number of possibilities ranging from
the choice of running their plant differently from an
efficiency standpoint because they may be shifting more their
demand from coal to natural gas or some other generation source
that they have chosen. There are technologies like CCS that
they may choose. There are fuel, there are technology switches.
There are new technologies that they may be adding. As a matter
fact, some are looking at retiring some asset and bringing some
new assets online. Obviously, nuclear energy and other things
will be in play, so----
Mr. Edwards. Stop using coal is the plan?
Mr. Regan. No. I think that the plan offers controlled
technologies that will reduce emissions if a plant decides to
continue with coal.
Mr. Edwards. Yeah. With all due respect, I just asked the
question what would be your instructions to them to reduce
emissions by 90 percent, and in your explanation you said
switch to natural gas and nuclear and some of these other
things. That effectively is an order to not use----
Mr. Regan. There is a coal plant in Texas right now using
CCS technology. What I don't want to do is what you are asking
me to do, which is tell them what to do. I think we have given
them a suite of options. We have talked to a lot of these
utility CEOs. There are a number of ways that they can meet the
emission reductions that we have laid out. Without being too
prescriptive, I think the rule lays out a plethora of options
and routes that they could choose.
Mr. Edwards. One more question, Mr. Chair, if I may. The

Mr. Regan. Yeah. I will say that you and I are probably

I think we have seen that with some other pesticides and
herbicides that, quite frankly, many farmers rely on.

Mr. Kilmer. Thanks, Chairman, and thank you for being back

Mr. Kilmer. I really like this job. I like when we get to

issues change. I still remember, I think, in my first term,
university researchers in my State showed a video of salmon
dying when exposed to storm water runoff, and they weren't
entirely sure why. Then as time has progressed, they were able
to distill it down and realize that it was a chemical in tires,
that, when it wore down, it became basically poisonous to fish,
and it was called 6PPDQ. I feel like now I get an advanced
degree in chemistry or something, but it is fatal to several
salmon species. Last year, we had several Tribes in our region,
including the Port Gamble's S'Klallam and Puyallup in my
district that petitioned the EPA to regulate 6PPDQ under the
Toxic Substances Control Act, and I appreciate EPA granting
that petition. I am glad that you are committed to working
across the Federal Government and with State agencies to
understand how 6PPDQ gets into our water and what we can do to
keep it out.

Mr. Simpson. Recognizing the far-reaching impacts of this
rule, the fiscal year 2024 House interior bill included a

until their concerns are addressed. Are you concerned that this
rule will prohibit new economic growth throughout the country

a solid, fair plan on how to handle events like wildfires and
prescribed burns, and tell us what your coordination has been
with the States thus far and what you are planning for the
future.
Mr. Regan. Well, thank you for that. I will say that
hopefully it is not a surprise, but maybe it is. You know, the
earliest that it can come into place is around 2032. We believe
that 99 percent of all the counties will meet the new standard.
The reason we believe that, the first is, is that we have got
an incredible working relationship with a lot of our State
environmental regulators, and so there are resources coming
from both the Bipartisan Infrastructure Law and the Inflation
Reduction Act that is going to infuse some capital in to help
with some of these PM concerns that we have.
The second is we have absolutely listened to our Western
States and our Western governors and taken a lot of advice on,
from a data standpoint, how we calculate the monitored data
from wildfires and from prescribed burns. We have a great
strategy for differentiating that particulate matter data that
is coming from a very sophisticated air monitoring system that
points to wildfires, prescribed burns, and those emissions that
we are actually targeting. I believe with the infusion of
capital from IRA, from listening to some of the concerns that
we have heard from some of the western governors, and looking
at my conversations with the Secretary of Commerce and the
President and others, who want us to continue to be a beacon of
light for jobs and creativity, I think we can meet that goal.
Mr. Simpson. Just remember that prescribed burns are how
you prevent, hopefully, in the future, catastrophic wildfires.
If we put too many restrictions on prescribed burns, you are
increasing the chances of catastrophic wildfires.
I am very concerned about losing fire retardant as a tool
to fight wildfires. Our firefighters should not be out in the

continued use of fire retardant?
Mr. Regan. Absolutely. This is a conversation that
Secretary Vilsack and I together have been looking at and
monitoring. I will say that we both want to be very clear that
we do not want to take any tools away from our firefighters,
and so we will not do that.
The first is, the EPA and the Forest Service have a signed
Facilities Compliance Agreement that allows the Forest Service
to continue fighting fires exactly the way they are so that we
can be on the right side of the law. We are working with USDA
and the Forest Service and looking at how we can issue a Clean
Water Act permit as soon as possible. That is very clear and
concise and really in a very transparent way demonstrates that
the Forest Service can use these tools and do it in a way that
is protective of the Clean Water Act.
Mr. Simpson. I appreciate that. I am not opposed to trying

Mr. Regan. Agree.

and at first, I was like, well, okay, fine, that is just, you

estimated that 100 billion garments are produced annually, most
of which end up in incinerators, generating about 17 million
tons of textile municipal solid waste. So 85 percent of the

sometimes is plastics in the ocean because of the synthetics
that they are made of, but right now, our National Recycling

would the EPA need to make a significant impact?
Mr. Regan. Well, thank you for this. I was telling you, I
believe, a while back that fast fashion wasn't something that
was on the tip of my tongue either. As I have traveled the
country and traveled the State, this is something that
especially young people are adamantly focused on, and so we are
taking this very serious. We released our part one of the
National Recycling Strategy in 2021, and as you pointed out, it
did not have enough in that focused on this specific topic, so
we have gone to work since then. I will say that we anticipate
releasing new data specifically on textile waste and recycling
this year, so that is on the way.
I would just say that in our budget, we are making a
request so that we can focus on these waste issues, these new

that contribute to methane, that contribute to climate change.

Ms. Pingree. Right.
Mr. Regan [continuing]. It is not very easy just to say

Ms. Pingree. Great. Well, thank you. I look forward to

Mr. Regan. Absolutely.
Ms. Pingree. I want to bring up one other waste topic that
I have been working on for quite a long time, and that is food
waste. Once again, the first time I heard about this several
years ago, I thought, okay, good. Everybody's grandmother told
them not to waste food. Of course, you shouldn't, but it turns
out we waste somewhere around 30 to 40 percent of the food in
this country. It is not only food that doesn't go to people who
are actually hungry, that doesn't get to the right places, but
it is also the land that we are using, the water that we are
wasting, the transportation. It starts to add up to a pretty
significant environmental issue, not to mention the same thing.
When food ends up in a landfill, it turns into methane gas, and
so you are just contributing once more to some of the
challenges we have around climate change.
I appreciated your Agency extending the comment deadline so
more groups could provide feedback on the national food waste
draft strategy that you are doing with the USDA and FDA. Can
you tell us a little bit more about the timeline for finalizing
the strategy, and can you talk more about how EPA will further
support efforts to reduce food waste?
Mr. Regan. Well, we were excited to expand that timeline
because we received so many comments. That gave us more time to
process those comments, and in that timeframe we expanded, we
got new comments. That being said, time is of essence, and so
we are hoping to have the final strategy out later this summer.
That direction will be out, and it will be stamped by USDA,
FDA, and EPA.
We also intend to invest around $83 million of the first
round of bill funding under the Solid Waste Infrastructure for
Recycling Grants Program to support programs that are directly
focused on organic recycling, composting, and anaerobic
digestion. We have got that strategy coming out, and we have
that $83 million that is focused solely on this issue.
Ms. Pingree. That is great because I know a lot of
municipalities would like to move towards composting their
solid waste, take it out of the waste stream, reduce the cost
to those municipalities, but they often need assistance or

Wednesday, May 1, 2024.

FISCAL YEAR 2025 REQUEST FOR THE INDIAN HEALTH SERVICE

WITNESSES

HON. ROSELYN TSO, DIRECTOR, INDIAN HEALTH SERVICE
JILLIAN CURTIS, DIRECTOR OF THE OFFICE OF FINANCE AND ACCOUNTING,
INDIAN HEALTH SERVICE
Mr. Simpson. Good morning, everyone. I would like to extend
a warm welcome to the Indian Health Service director, Roselyn
Tso. Thank you for your leadership at IHS and for being with us
today to discuss the administration's fiscal year 2025 Budget
Request. We were all really looking forward to Congressman Tom
Cole joining us today in his new role as the full committee
chair. However, the devastating tornados across Oklahoma have
required that he stay back in his district, and without
objection I ask that his prepared remarks be included in the
record.
[The information follows:]
[GRAPHIC(S) NOT AVAILABLE IN TIFF FORMAT]

Mr. Simpson. Chairman Cole is the first Native American to
ever chair the Appropriations Committee, and he has made it his
legacy in Congress to advocate for Indian Country and ensure
that Tribes receive the resources they need to take care of
their communities.
Under both Republican and Democratic leadership, this
committee has continued to prioritize and support healthcare
services for American Indians and Alaska Natives, and as you
look across here, we have Chairwoman Pingree and we have former
chairwoman of this committee, Betty McCollum. Ken Calvert was
chairman here. All of us have put a priority on Indian Health
Services and the needs and trying to keep our treaty
responsibilities that we have. I have no doubt that Chairman
Cole will continue to advance this critical bipartisan effort
as he leads this committee. There is no one better for that
job, and I as well as Ranking Member Pingree stand by ready to
help.
In 2023, Congress provided advanced appropriations for the
Indian Health Service for the first time, and that was because
of the tireless work of Chairwoman Pingree along with the rest
of the committee. She was the leader in making sure that we got
that done, and I thank you for that. Advanced appropriations
provide the Indian Health service with the certainty needed to
properly serve communities, provide stability for healthcare
providers, and improve long-term planning for services, even
during continuing resolutions and government shutdowns. I know
this funding does not begin to meet the full need, but it is a
step in the right direction, and it is a signal of our
understanding and commitment to upholding our trust and treaty
responsibilities.
For fiscal year 2025, the President's budget requests $7.96
billion in discretionary funding for IHS, a 14-percent increase
over the fiscal year 2024 level. Notably, the request does not
include advanced appropriations but instead requests that all
funding for IHS be moved to mandatory starting in fiscal year
2026. I will continue to advocate to provide certainty to
healthcare professionals and programs in Indian Country, but I
do have some technical questions about shifting IHS programs to
mandatory, so in the meantime, I look forward to hearing how
advanced appropriations can continue to support planning and
operations of IHS programs.
Director Tso, your leadership at IHS is vital to not only
upholding our trust and treaty agreements, but ensuring
adequate and accessible healthcare to all Tribal members. Thank
you again for joining us this morning. I look forward to our
discussion. Now I would like to yield to Ranking Member Pingree
for her opening statement.
Ms. Pingree. Thank you very much, Mr. Chair, and good
morning, Director. Thank you for being with us today to discuss
the fiscal year 2025 budget request for the Indian Health
Service. The Indian Health Service provides healthcare to
approximately 2.8 million American Indians and Alaska Natives
to a network of over 600 hospitals, clinics, and health
stations on or near Indian reservations. With 70 percent of
Alaska Natives currently living in urban areas, the IHS funds
41 healthcare organizations in urban areas.
IHS relies on annual discretionary appropriations for its
funding, and sadly this has not kept pace with need. The
statistics on health disparities in Indian Country are
sobering. This subcommittee has been dedicated to finding
solutions, both through investments in the IHS and through
improvements in how funding is delivered, namely through the
enactment of advanced appropriations. It is critical we
continue our efforts.
Your budget requests discretionary funding in fiscal year
2025 and proposes a shift to mandatory funding beginning in
fiscal year 2026. Though I note that mandatory funding is the
jurisdiction of the authorizers, I am interested to understand
how this would improve the health and well-being of American
Indians and Alaska Natives. I look forward to discussing your
request and know under the leadership of Chairman Simpson, this
subcommittee will continue to work in a bipartisan fashion to
honor the United States' treaty responsibilities to Tribal
nations.
Director Tso, thank you again for appearing before us this
morning. I appreciate your testimony and answers to our
questions. I yield back.
Mr. Simpson. Director Tso.
Ms. Tso. Good morning, Chairman Simpson, and Ranking Member
Pingree, and members of the committee. Thank you for the
opportunity to testify on the President's fiscal year 2025
budget request for the Indian Health Service. Before I turn to
the budget, I first want to acknowledge and thank each of you
[Audio malfunction] advanced appropriations. IHS made 100
percent of payments to eligible Tribes through compacts and
contracts in the first 2 weeks of the fiscal year.
I also appreciate the opportunity to share with you the
steps I have taken as the IHS director to improve transparency,
accountability, and oversight of the Agency. As we seek
additional funding and new funding authorities, it is crucial
that the Indian Health Service improve its internal operations
to ensure safe, quality healthcare and protect and support its
relationships with Tribes and urban Indian organizations. To
ensure that I fully understand the challenges and
accomplishments throughout Indian Country, I value the
opportunity to visit with Tribal leaders across Indian Country
to gain their input and perspective.
To move the Indian Health Service to a high-performing
organization, I implemented the first Agency work plan in
January of 2023 that achieves three major accomplishments
including total system safety strategies, the IHS patient
safety policy, and improving Purchase and Referred Care
Program, such as establishing new medical priorities,
establishing a financial monitoring system and staff training,
and to obligate PRC funding in a timely manner. Our 2024 Agency
work plan outlines steps that IHS has taken to address
priorities as well as mitigate risks. The Agency work plan
addresses a wide range of issues, including patient safety,
human capital, operations, finance, and compliance. This work
builds on our effort to meet the GAO's criteria for removal
from its High Risk List.
With your support, the IHS budget has grown 69 percent in
the last decade. We know this type of growth is challenging to
accomplish in a constrained discretionary funding environment.
Over the years, the work with our Tribes and urban partners
underscore our shared goal to improve health outcomes for all
American Indians and Alaska Natives. With this shared goal in
mind, the administration acknowledges the 2025 budget request
for Indian Health Service.
We know that despite our shared efforts, IHS is still
underfunded. This underfunding of the Indian healthcare system
directly contributes to the stark health disparities in our
Tribal communities. American Indians and Alaska Natives born
today have an average life expectancy that is 10.9 years fewer
than U.S. all races population. Longstanding health disparities
were complicated by the pandemic, with American Indians and
Alaska Natives experiencing disproportionate rates of COVID-19
infections, hospitalizations, and deaths. Addressing these
inequities is a moral imperative for our Nation, and it will
require bold actions from all of us to ensure we are upholding
our commitments to Indian Country. That is why the budget
proposes to build on the enactment of advanced appropriations
by funding the IHS as discretionary in fiscal year 2025.
Beginning in fiscal year 2026, the budget will move to all
funding to IHS mandatory. The administration continues to stand
behind mandatory funding as the most appropriate long-term
funding solution for IHS. We know it is a significant change
that will take time and collaborations with Congress and Tribes
to accomplish. We believe that a change of this magnitude is
overdue, and we must deliver for Indian Country. As we work
towards securing stable, predictable, and adequate funding to
meet the needs of Indian Country, we are committed to working
closely with our stakeholders.
Again, thank you for the opportunity to speak with you
today, and I am happy to answer any questions the committee may
have.
[The statement of Ms. Tso follows:]
[GRAPHIC(S) NOT AVAILABLE IN TIFF FORMAT]

Mr. Simpson. Thank you, Director. Let me ask a couple
questions first. As I said, Chairman Cole was looking forward
to being here today and wanted to ask about changes to contract
support costs, and he asked me to ask a couple of questions. I
am going to ask those because he is my chairman. How does the
IHS plan to go about developing an accurate estimate for the
Tribal contract support costs that are associated with Tribal
third-party funded operations serving Native American patients
and other IHS beneficiaries? Will there be Tribal consultation
involved?
Ms. Tso. Thank you for that question. Absolutely we have to
integrate Tribal consultation into any work that we do at the
Indian Health Service, particularly of this magnitude for CSC
on third party. With regards to that, IHS already has
established methodologies for calculating CSC today. We are
building on that to try to make sure that we incorporate what
our strategies will be, depending on the decision that is
forthcoming from the Supreme Court. We are prepared to have
those conversations as soon as possible with Tribes as we move
forward.
Mr. Simpson. Okay. We have both consistently heard from
Tribes about critical problems resulting from staffing issues
across the Indian Health Services, especially in the Purchased
and Referred Care Program. Director, what is IHS doing to
address staffing shortages and the subsequent problems, delays,
and financial impacts resulting from these shortages and
barriers to hiring and retaining employees as I just
identified, and what is being done to address that?
Ms. Tso. Thank you, again, for that question. If there is
one thing that keeps me up at night, it is our workforce, how
fragile it is today. That is the same thing across the Nation
in our healthcare system that we are all being stretched to the
limits with regard to staffing. At the Indian Health Service, I
have taken great efforts to try to address this issue. Some of
the examples that we are doing right now, as I see the
challenges growing every day, I was out in an Indian Country
last week and heard from one healthcare facility where we have
17 vacancies for pharmacists, and we know the value of our
pharmacists and the work that they do in any healthcare
facility. In IHS, pharmacists do a lot more than go beyond just
dispensing medication. They actually do patient care. In this
particular space, having those kinds of vacancies creates these
risks that we are talking about, and we have to get in front of
that.
I have been working very closely with the Commissioned
Corps, the Public Health Service at HHS, to make sure that we
maximize all of these resources that could come to any country
as we move forward and taking strategies about how do we keep
the staff that we have at the Indian Health Service. In this
particular space, we are making some strides here with
Commissioned Corps and making sure that, again, we have what we
need.
We are also taking a whole approach at Indian Health
Service to streamline HR. We have 12 regions within IHS, and so
o before, every region was sort of doing their own thing. We
are not big enough to be able to continue that kind of an
operation. We have to bring our resources together, and
bringing resources together to be able to address the system
wide issue that we have is critical right now. We are in that
space right now to try to figure out and what we are learning.
Again, as you can imagine that 12 different organizations
trying to do the same thing, we have inequities and we have
disparities and we have inconsistencies. It is hard to
understand if you are even building towards success, so looking
at that and helping us to try to figure out how do we maximize
resources. In that one example in there is we are taking the
workload and lining it up with the workforce instead of trying
to figure out who is doing what and so forth.
Building this alignment, and we did this, I used the same
strategy for PRC, the same strategy for the bill work that we
need to do. In addition to everything that we are doing, as I
shared yesterday, all of the work that we are doing for the
infrastructure bill $3.5 billion that Indian Health Service
received about 3 years ago. I need 500 engineers today to
complete that work. Everybody needs engineers, too, because a
lot of other people got infrastructure dollars. How do we do
that? I have written a letter to every engineering school
across the Nation to let them know, number one, about Indian
Health Service; number two, here are the ways that we can get
you into the Indian healthcare system as we move forward to do
the work that we need to do. We have also looked at additional
strategies there. I am also working very closely with Tribal
schools and Tribal colleges to let them know, again, we are a
workforce that needs you. How do we do that?
Taking these different strategies and running with them as
fast as we can to make sure that, again, we have to tell the
story about IHS, we have to also let people know that we are
simplifying and streamlining our process so it is not so
complicated to come into a Federal system. Additionally, we
have looked at some administrative changes as well as we have
requested A-19s that help us streamline. I will give you a
couple of examples in that space that with regards to loan
repayment, for example, or scholarships.
If you look at within just HHS, you have IHS that has a
loan repayment program, and then you have HRSA that has a loan
repayment program. HRSA's money is not taxed. IHS' money is
taxed. If I am an applicant, I am probably going to go look at
HRSA first because I am not going to be taxed. These kinds of
discrepancies and these kinds of inequities continue to
minimize our efforts and our work at Indian Health Service.
Then lastly, there are authorities. Working very closely
with my partners at the Veterans Administration, they have
maximum Title 38 and maximum Title 5 with respect to hiring and
retention of their staff, and those are the kinds of
authorities that IHS needs as well. We need to make sure that
we have the ability to compete with the VA, and right now we
kind of just have our staff going from IHS to the VA, from the
VA to IHS, depending on that, and that is not addressing the
inequities for the people out in any country.
There are a number of things that we are looking at, and
there are a number of things that we also know that need to be
adjusted on [Audio malfunction.]
Mr. Simpson. Yeah, I don't know what it is, but I do have
one final question from Chairman Cole. It is about something
that both he and I are very interested in, the development of a
behavior health pilot program. We all recognize that there is a
growing need to increase support for behavior health programs
in Indian Health Services. Each year, Tribes are realizing a
greater need to provide services for prevention, treatment, and
recovery from mental health and substance abuse disorders. What
can the Agency do to help address this growing need and what
additional resources are needed in fiscal year 2025 to ensure
Tribal members can receive the quality care and behavior health
services they need?
Ms. Tso. Yes, yes, absolutely. At the Indian Health
Service, this is definitely an area that probably on every
single day we are looking at strategies about mental health,
behavior health, but we are looking more beyond that. We look
at all the social determinants of health that our people deal
with, and when I travel out to Indian Country, when I meet with
Tribal leaders, food security is always at the top of the list.
Transportation, being able to get from point A to point B, is a
problem. Looking at all of this holistically to try to find out
how do we address these issues because, particularly with
patients that need mental health, behavior health services, if
there are other factors that are impacting their ability to get
the care that they need and moreover to seek the services, our
window of opportunity is so small when these patients are ready
to get care, so looking at all of the social determinants of
health is absolutely critical.
Then in this particular space is facility infrastructure
that I am hearing from Tribes all the time that, yes, Indian
Health Service focuses on hospitals and clinics. Those
treatment facilities, those detox facilities are becoming so
much more important to try to address, to get people into a
facility and a facility close to their home that they can get
the services that they need, so in this particular space, we
know that there are additional resources that need to be had.
In addition to that, we also know approximately 60 percent of
the Indian Health Service has been contracted or compacted by
Tribes, which means that they have taken the resources from IHS
and they operate their own healthcare system. In that space, it
makes it even more of a need there is how do we now look at the
resources that we have at the Indian Health Service, and really
the 40 percent that we have left and then the 60 percent that
additional resources need to be had to address facility
infrastructure.
We track now, on a regular basis, the issues that we have
with opioid in our IHS healthcare facilities, and we have seen
a huge reduction in that by looking at different strategies of
how we can address this. On a regular basis, we also deploy
services and support and resources when we have suicide
clusters that we hear from time to time. Our system is now
better able to address some of these things that are happening
in real time that allows us to, even in spite of the limited
resources of staff that we have, that we now have teams
available that can go out to any Tribal or our IHS healthcare
facility to help with these crises that are happening. There is
definitely the facility side is huge on IHS.
We are looking at the joint venture model that has been
extremely successful in Indian Country. Joint venture, again,
is where the Tribe builds the facility and then the government
staffs and equips that facility and what can we do in this
particular area because we can't just focus on the healthcare
side without looking, again, at the behavior health and mental
health needs. Looking at facilities and how can we spread that
to look at those opportunities to look at facility
infrastructure. There is a huge need in this area.
I will also say the vacancy rates that I have at any health
service today, which is the 40 percent that remains, is at 40
percent. I have a 40-percent vacancy rate for mental health and
behavior health. I cannot address healthcare needs in Indian
Country [Audio malfunction] of challenges that we have with the
workforce. Really requires us to be very creative, looking at
telehealth, telemedicine, and looking at what we are learning
more and more about is the community health aid program, which
is a program that was born in Alaska where we train individuals
that are part of the community, that can then help us
strategize and be able to address the needs in each local
community.
Mr. Simpson. Thank you for the answers. Ms. Pingree.
Ms. Pingree. Let me take off from this issue about your
vacancies, and thank you for explaining to us the sort of
comprehensive way you are going about this. One thing I wanted

for those Tribal communities, by the Tribal communities, and
utilizing, again, our elders all the way down to the young
people. How do we do this? That is what I see. I have seen that
absolutely change over the years, and making those funds
permanent, I think, is absolutely something that I have seen
the data, I have seen the progress. Probably one of the best
examples in Indian Health Service about prevention of diabetes,
prevention of heart disease, again, all of that equates to less
cost, less hospital visits, and those kinds of things.
Mr. Harder. It is great to see some of the evidence around
how that saves us money. You know, an ounce of prevention is
worth a pound of cure., and I think the Special Diabetes
Program is estimated to save Medicare over $500 million over
the next 10 years. Congratulations on the good work and on all
the evidence that backs up that it is working. What is the
breakdown of how that program is operating in the field? Do you
have a sense of how much is on prevention, how much is on
treatment? What are the programs that have really had the
highest ROI, and what do you think the unmet need is out there
today?
Ms. Tso. Thank you, again, for that question. With respect
to the Diabetes Program and majority of those funds are going
to Tribally operated programs, so, again, each Tribal community
has to have the resources they need to build what is best for
them. Again, not the government or not Indian Health Service
saying it needs to be this way or that way. Those decisions are
made locally in terms of what do they need to do to, as I said,
very creative things in terms of just basic things of upgrading
their kitchen so they can do more education, for example. What
can they do in this space of weight loss? What are the
activities there? What are opportunities that they can do in
their infrastructure?
Again, many of our facilities don't have the infrastructure
where people can go to exercise, if you will, or go to places.
Especially in Minnesota, it gets very cold there, there has got
to be a location for people to go because you can't just tell
people go exercising, go move around outside. It is just not
practical. Again, Tribal governments making decisions for their
people of what is best is so important.
Majority of the diabetes funds goes out to Indian Country,
probably, I would say, 90 percent. The remaining is associated
with our IHS-operated programs. That 40 percent I was talking
about, that is still IHS, so very important. I see Tribal
governments, Tribal leaders, Tribal communities making the
decisions for themselves and their community because we are not
all the same is what works best.
Mr. Harder. Thank you for that, Director, and one last
question. Do you see any unmet need? Are there applications
that are going unfilled? How do you quantify how much demand
there is for this program that you are not able to meet with
the current budget?
Ms. Tso. Yes. The one thing that I would see as a barrier
is that this funding is going out in grants. I understand that
there is always a need to make sure that the money is being
spent wisely, but if the money were to go out in either our
contracts or compacts or a different method, again, that gives
them more flexibility to do that. When you are running a grant,
then you are sort of having to limit what you think you can do
versus what I have seen other Tribes do when we do give them
the resources, so they make those choices. There is more
flexibility there. The unmet need is still that we still have
diabetes numbers going up, we still have opportunity to build
those prevention programs that have not quite been met by the
budget that we have.
Mr. Harder. Thank you. I yield back.
Mr. Simpson. Thank you. Something came up that was kind of
interesting that you mentioned. You said that the Navajo Nation
reserve seats at University of Arizona Medical School?
Ms. Tso. Yes.
Mr. Simpson. That is important that we get Tribal members
into medical school, dental schools, nursing schools, those
kinds of things because they are more likely to come back to
their reservation and provide those services, so that helps
with the staffing issue that we have around here, and it got me
thinking. In Idaho, we have programs called WICHE and WWAMI.
One of them is for dental students, one of them is for medical
schools. We can't afford a medical school or a dental school,
so we reserve many seats at the University of Washington, as
does Alaska, Montana, Idaho. Then at the dental school at Case
Western in Omaha, we reserve many seats for Idaho dental
students, and the State pays for the out-of-State tuition when
a student gets accepted to those programs, and most of the
time, those students graduate and come back to Idaho. It is a
lot cheaper and makes sense for us to do that rather than try
to go out and build a medical school. Does IHS do anything like
that, or is this individual Tribes doing it?
Ms. Tso. A majority of the time, it is individual Tribes
that are doing that, making those types of commitments that we
do, but we also pay for long-term training for some of our
providers if they want to go. Like, if we have a dentist and he
or she wants to get advanced schooling and whatever, we do pay
for that. In return, they stay with us for a longer period of
time, so there are those opportunities on that.
I know that we work very closely with the University of
Washington. We can do more to make sure that we are tied in
very closely with these universities out there. Our unit, OHSU
out in Portland, Oregon, Oregon Health Sciences University,
those are the ones that I know. I also realize that many of our
Tribes and our Tribal organizations are already tied in with
these universities that are close to them, so they can tap
these resources that are available.
Mr. Simpson. Well, and for a large Tribe like the Navajo
Nation or some of the others, that would probably be easier to
do, but you have got some small Tribes that probably don't know
how to go about that. I think that is something we ought to
explore. Talking to medical and dental schools, nursing
schools, and seeing if I can be active in reserving seats for
Tribal members at some of these schools because I think that
would help us address our staffing issue.
Now, I want to ask another question that I told you I was
going to ask. This is not a criticism of IHS or anything else,
but I have heard from several Tribes that they believe we would
do better to eliminate IHS and give direct appropriations to
the various Tribes. While that might work for some Tribes, it
might not for other Tribes because all Tribes have different
needs and they have different sizes and different abilities.
What I hear is that because of the overhead costs that IHS has,
they could do better delivering the services directly to their
Tribal members if they had a direct appropriation. That might
be true for some and not for others.
My question is, and I am not prejudging this. I am trying
to find out. I want the same thing you want, as does this
committee and the Tribes want. That is to deliver the best
healthcare that we can for the dollars that we have to Indians
across this country. Is this model the best model? What is your
overhead cost, and is there a different model that we ought to
be looking at? I am not suggesting that we would do it this
year or anything else. I am looking at the next 10 years about
how is the best way to do this, so I would appreciate your
thoughts on that.
Ms. Tso. Thank you for that, and I thought about this a
little bit after I left your office yesterday. A couple of
things that I would say is certainly the Indian Self-
Determination Act, as I said, 60 percent of these Tribes have
made those decisions and say we are going to run our own
healthcare program. A majority of those funds or most of those
funds that they have taken from the Indian Health Service, 40
percent today still say, we want IHS to continue to----
Mr. Simpson. Let me ask you on that. If you were a Tribe
that you were upset with Indian Health Services and you said,
we can run our program better and cheaper than going to IHS,
could they choose----
Mr. Simpson. To go outside of IHS and deliver the services
themselves?
Ms. Tso. Sixty percent already do.
Mr. Simpson. Okay.
Ms. Tso. Yeah. The Indian Self-Determination allows them to
do that. Then so what remains are the dollars that support the
remaining 40 percent of the Tribes. As they continue to choose
to go on their own and be self-determined, then IHS will keep
shrinking like it is right now. The overhead costs that we are
talking about here is minimal in terms of the 40 percent of the
dollars that we have left, a majority of those are supporting
these healthcare facilities that we have out in the Indian
Country. Those are staffing dollars. Of course, as always, the
majority of the funds that IHS is asking for, staffing dollars
and operation of healthcare facilities, so that is already
happening.
Eventually, you know, the theory was way back when these
conversations were had in the late 80s and early 90s, was that
eventually Tribes would be operating all of their own
healthcare system. At that point, then you would have only what
we will call residual or what are federally inherent functions
that remain of the IHS. I believe, to some degree, we are
already going that direction.
Mr. Simpson. Okay.

Mr. Simpson. Yes.
Ms. McCollum. I hear what you are saying, and I will just

Ms. Tso. Absolutely. Thank you for that. Again, I will add
to that, is that in many of these very rural locations,
particularly in your area because I visited up there, we are
seeing where the other facilities or other healthcare systems
are not successful. Our Tribal health programs have now become
the safety net in some of these areas. They are seeing
everybody--everybody--in those communities, not just Tribal
people. They are seeing more and more people because they are
the only game in town, if you will.
With regards to transportation, this is huge for us right
now. I have met with the Department of Transportation and
looking at a whole Federal approach to what is it that we can
do to address this where we know that they have programs that
Tribes can tap into in terms of maybe transportation systems in
Tribal communities and so forth, and then how do we leverage
that? How do we make sure, again, that people can get what they
need? Moreover, as I was talking about earlier, the
improvements that we have made to purchased and referred care,
we have now integrated travel into that because it wasn't there
before, so we are bringing, again, more flexibilities that
Tribes have the latitude to do what they need to do. We learned
this as a result of COVID when we were flying people out, and
we are just sending people all over the country but no way to
bring them back when they were ready to come home. That was a
whole gap in our system.
We are trying to look at how do we at least provide that
flexibility for Tribal communities to make sure that people can
travel here and there. We have even gone as far as I looked at
how do we address ambulance, for example, EMS program services,
almost one of our lowest funded programs, but has become so
critical in Indian Country. When we talk about fentanyl, when
we talk about these issues, are the safety issues for
everybody, not just the patient, but the drivers and all of
that. How are we looking at that more holistically to make sure
that we are doing everything we can? The transportation, again,
I will go back to Alaska, where they have told me that they
don't either have flights anymore or they have been
significantly reduced, so how do you take a patient from point
A to point B for the services that they need?
Trying to look at all of the strategies of looking at the
whole healthcare system, not just Indian Health Service or not
just the BIA, but Department of Interior, EPA, all of these
programs is what I have been working with, to say, hey, there
has got to be a full government approach to Indian Country when
we are talking about health. I think taking a little bit
different look at how we do that instead of everybody being in
their own, whatever, is what we need to do so everyone is aware
of this.
Ms. McCollum. Love it. Thank you, Mr. Chair.
Mr. Simpson. Thank you. All I am trying to do by asking

One other question. Modernization of the electronic
healthcare record system continues to be an extremely
significant cost driver for IHS. The President's budget request
includes $245 million increase for the total of $435 million
for fiscal year 2025 for electronic healthcare records. That is
more than all dental health programs, all mental health
programs, and all alcohol and substance abuse programs and
nearly doubled all preventative healthcare programs combined.
Can you please justify this investment? How will this help
provide better healthcare in Indian Country?
As you know, Veterans Affairs has endured significant
challenges transitioning to this new system that have
contributed to patient safety risks. What is IHS doing to
provide timely and adequate training to prevent some of the
issues that the VA has faced during this transition? What
conversations have you had with VA to learn from their
challenges?
Ms. Tso. Thank you again. Another very important topic at
the Indian Health Service. You know, I understand and hear that
this is a huge price tag for the Indian Health Service, but the
price tag of patient care and safety and all of those things
are equally important as we are moving forward with this. When
we look at electronic health records, you know, our system is
more than 40 years old, so while we have made investments over
the years, we have never made the kind of change that we need
to make today.
Again, when we are talking about complexities of healthcare
system across the country, let alone Indian Country and where
we have very remote locations where we have limited
connectivity at times and all of these other challenges,
finding a way to allow us to move forward in this space. The
$27 million decrease in 2024 was a huge hit to us because we
are on a schedule, we want to make sure that we deliver to
Indian Country the best healthcare system before we are
documenting patient care. Why is it so important? Our veterans
now, you know, there is no easy way when a veteran comes into
an IHS facility, and they have to go to a VA facility, that
information is not being shared.
When we talk about the opioid issues and mental health
issues, there is no way to transfer to say, hey, this is a
patient that saw me yesterday, I am now referring them to you.
It is mind-boggling to think that we don't have enough of a
system that allows us to do this. What I have seen Tribes do,
and probably most of us experienced that, we can text with our
provider. Our system doesn't allow us to do that. We need to
move to a system that allows us to have more direct contact
with our providers or with our patients.
With regards to the cost, you know, we want to stay on
schedule with the cost. The money that we are asking for, I
realize, is a lot, but it is a huge game changer for Indian
Country. We have been working very closely with all of our
Tribes to make sure that they are informed of every step that
we are taking. Moreover, that award that we made late last year
to General Dynamics was not challenged by anyone, unlike some
of these challenges that you raised about the VA, and so we are
learning from the VA. We are working very closely with the VA
in some of the things that they did that we have done totally
different. We went out on a wide competition to find the best
contractor that we could have, a VA just went with this all
sources. That is one of the challenges that they have.
We have communicated broadly with our partners across any
country of what we need, what will it look like. We are not
going to agree to make any changes to the electronic health
record until it is in place. We are not looking at trying to,
you know, come up with something that we have to live with
forever. We want to make sure that what we build is going to
work for us, and then we can look at how we make changes down
the road as we move forward.
Mr. Simpson. You would think it would be relatively simple.

Your proposal is for mandatory funding for Indian Health
Services for coming years. We did advanced appropriations and
that, the main reason behind that was so when there was a

everything else, we didn't shut down Indian Health Services. To

I guess my question is, mandatory funding at what level?

Ms. Tso. Thank you for that question. Again, first of all,
I cannot say enough about advanced appropriations, which is a
game changer for us this year to be able to, again, have Tribes
in IHS be able to plan and have a predictable budget, but we
believe that mandatory will grow for us the way that we need it
to grow. It is already built in for inflationary costs that are
in there and the expenses that we need for staffing and those
sorts of things. It is critical in that area that we believe
the growth over the 10-year period will be significant and more
than what we are going to see in the discretionary funding
source. We are needing that. We are needing the mandatory
funding.
When I talk with Tribal leaders across Indian Country and
even within HHS, Tribal consultation, the price tag that Tribes
tell me is that $63 billion is what we need to fully fund the
Indian Health Service. The gap is large and huge when we talk
about this, but it is also very real. When I travel out to
Indian Country, when I meet with Tribes on just how do we do
this differently? How do we do it better to be able to meet the
needs of our people? We believe, again, because of the
automatic growth that is incorporated into mandatory funding,
that that will be what will build that foundation for Indian
healthcare across Indian Country.
Mr. Simpson. Well, I don't know how they came up with $63

Ms. Curtis. Sure. You know, I think the point you are

Mr. Simpson. Okay. Well, I appreciate that, and I know that
