Wednesday, May 1, 2024.

FISCAL YEAR 2025 REQUEST FOR THE ARTS AND HUMANITIES

WITNESSES

KAYWIN FELDMAN, DIRECTOR, NATIONAL GALLERY OF ART
DR. MARIA ROSARIO JACKSON, CHAIR, NATIONAL ENDOWMENT FOR THE ARTS
SHELLY C. LOWE (Navajo), CHAIR, NATIONAL ENDOWMENT FOR THE HUMANITIES
Mr. Simpson. Oh, I am supposed to turn on the light or turn
on the mike, yes. Kaywin Feldman, director of the National
Gallery of Art, Dr. Maria Rosario Jackson, chair of the
National Endowment for the Arts, and Shelly Lowe, chair of the
National Endowment for the Humanities, thank you for being here
to discuss the budget priorities for your agencies for fiscal
year 2025. Originally, we also had the Smithsonian scheduled
and we were going to do two panels, but apparently the
administrator is not feeling well, and so I understand that.
Preserving our culture, art, and history is essential to
protecting the legacy of our Nation and preparing us for the
future. The National Gallery of Art is one of the most renowned
and visited institutions tasked with this responsibility. Each
year, millions of visitors, both in person and online, access
your collections. Through your exhibitions, educational, and
outreach programs, you encourage children and scholars across
the world to discover new knowledge, experience art, and
explore the world around them. The NEA and NEH are tasked with
similar responsibilities, bringing incredible opportunities
through art, history, and education to rural communities.
Whether it is healing through the arts, arts education,
research on artificial intelligence, or promoting historical
research amongst middle school and high school students in
preparation for the 250th anniversary of the signing of the
Declaration of Independence, I have seen firsthand the impact
that the NEA and NEH dollars have had in my district in Idaho.
For fiscal year 2025, the President has requested $250
million for the National Gallery, $210.1 million for the
National Endowment for the Arts, and $200 million for the
National Endowment for the Humanities. I appreciate the
opportunity to understand how the budget supports your
agencies' priorities and reasonably builds on the investments
Congress has made in 2024. We must ensure that the Gallery's
facilities are protected to reduce risks to the collections,
visitors, and employees, and that we continue to support the
NEA/NEH efforts to expand access to the arts and humanities to
all Americans. I look forward to our discussion and appreciate
you all being here and now yield to the ranking member,
Pingree.
Ms. Pingree. Thank you, Mr. Chair. I am so happy to have
you all here today to discuss the fiscal year 2025 budget
requests for arts and humanities agencies. I am lucky enough to
also be the chair of the Arts Caucus, and I know the arts have
an incredible value as a positive tool for economic
development, education, and community building.
I am very proud that over the last few years, we were able
to increase funding for the National Endowment for the Arts and
the National Endowment for the Humanities to historic highs,
and that we were able to protect their funding in the
challenging fiscal environment of fiscal year 2024. While I am
glad we protected it, it is still not enough. At $270 million
each, the endowments are small, but they are mighty
powerhouses, and this modest investment has a far-reaching
impact, with funding benefiting every single district in
America. I am sorry the Smithsonian wasn't able to participate
today, but I am so glad the National Gallery of Art is with us.
These institutions inspire, challenge, and educate us through
their collections.
We know that operating facilities in a constrained fiscal
environment is challenging, yet it is essential that we
continue to share the Smithsonian Institution and the National
Gallery collections with the Nation and the world and maintain
them for future generations. I am pleased one way the
Smithsonian Institution does this is through the Smithsonian
Affiliate Program that affords museums, such as the Abbe Museum
in Bar Harbor in my State, access to the Smithsonian resources.
I look forward to discussing the fiscal year 2025 budget
requests for the Arts, Humanities, and the National Gallery and
learning more about the goals you have outlined for fiscal year
2025. Thank you again, Director Feldman, Chair Lowe, and Chair
Jackson for appearing before us this afternoon. I appreciate
your testimony and your answers to our questions. Thank you,
and I yield back.
Mr. Simpson. Thank you. Director Feldman, we will start
with you.
Ms. Feldman. Chair Simpson, Ranking Member Pingree, members
of the subcommittee, thank you for the opportunity to testify
today. It is my great pleasure to talk to you about the
National Gallery of Art and address our fiscal year 2025
Federal budget request. I would also like to thank you for your
support of our fiscal year 2024 budget, which provided the
critical funds to keep the National Gallery operational as we
emerged from a very challenging time to set new attendance
records. In my time with you, I will share just a few examples
of how the National Gallery is serving growing audiences
regionally, nationally, with inspiring experiences made
possible by our beautiful facilities and the masterpieces that
we hold in trust for the Nation.
American generosity has been at the core of our identity
since the beginning. In 1936, in a very populous gesture, our
founder, Andrew W. Mellon, wrote to Franklin Delano Roosevelt,
suggesting that America needed a major museum to expose her
citizens to the best works of art across time. He proposed a
public-private partnership and offered to donate his collection
and the necessary funds to build a world-class museum. The gift
was accepted by the President and Congress with the pledge of
Federal support and the work began. Here on the table, you see
the original letter from FDR written on New Year's Day in 1937
accepting Mr. Mellon's offer for the American people. President
Roosevelt presided over the dedication of the museum on March
17th, 1941 when he stated that works of art matter because they
are ``symbols of the human spirit.'' I have to say it moves me
profoundly that during some of humanity's darkest hours during
the Second World War, the President and Congress recognized
that art was central to our democracy.
Last year, the National Gallery welcomed 3.8 million
visitors, making us the second most visited art museum
nationally and the eighth most visited globally. At our 25-acre
campus on the National Mall, all people are invited to explore
artistic wonders through our permanent collections and special
exhibitions.
A major attraction is our stunning portrait of Ginevra de'
Benci by Leonardo da Vinci. Painted 25 years before the Mona
Lisa, our portrait is Leonardo's only painting in the Americas
and is a treasure that people flock to see. In case you haven't
visited her lately, I do have, not the original. We couldn't
bring Ginevra today, but we are fortunate to have two masterful
drawings by Leonardo, one of which I have brought with me. His
``Grotesque Head of an Old Woman'' is one of several drawings
he made that explores human physiognomy. In these tiny
grotesque drawings, Leonardo combined real-life observation
with outlandish imagination, preparing him for the
unprecedented range and subtlety of human expression for which
he is so famous. Although this drawing is one of the smaller
works of art in our collection, it is among the most
significant and fascinating. All three works by Leonardo were
gifted to the Nation by patriotic Americans. Archival
materials, such as the FDR letter and any work of art on paper,
including Leonardo drawings, are part of our Make an
Appointment Program. Literally, anyone can make an appointment
to see these objects in person in our supervised study rooms.
Our commitment to inviting all people to explore and
experience art extends beyond our walls. To share one example,
our massive open online course, known as a MOOC, offers free
and dynamic virtual classroom experiences to educators of all
grade levels, subjects, and locations. Since the program's
launch in 2019, we have hosted nearly 37,000 participants
joining us from every single State, and because our collections
and resources belong to the American people, we have plans to
share more of our original works of art beyond our Washington
campus. Already a generous institutional lender, we will lend
more of our collection to museums located in communities
outside of major urban centers all across America with long-
term loans and exhibition partnerships.
Of course, none of this service to our Nation would be
possible without the support of this committee and Congress. I
am happy to answer your questions, expanding on our fiscal year
2025 budget request and our work to serve the American people.
I extend my heartfelt gratitude for your commitment to the
National Gallery of Art and its mission to serve the Nation by
welcoming all people to explore and experience art, creativity,
and our shared humanity. Thank you.
[The statement of Ms. Feldman follows:]
[GRAPHIC(S) NOT AVAILABLE IN TIFF FORMAT]

Mr. Simpson. Thank you, Director. Dr. Jackson.
Ms. Jackson. Good afternoon, Chairman Simpson, Ranking
Member Pingree, and distinguished members of subcommittee. It
is an honor to be with you today to discuss President Biden's
fiscal year 2025 request of $210.1 million for the National
Endowment for the Arts. Thank you for your tremendous support.
Separate from my written testimony, in the few minutes I have,
I would like to highlight key elements of how we are seeking to
fulfill our mission and why it matters.
The NEA is a small, but mighty agency. Our staff has worked
tirelessly to help make arts participation possible for all
Americans, and I am proud to work with such devoted public
servants. The art sector continues to be a remarkable economic
driver/contributor to the well-being of our communities, an
important element of our social fabric. The most recently
available data showed that in 2022, the arts and cultural
industries generated $1.1 trillion and supported 5.2 million
jobs. However, larger portions of the creative economy
throughout the country still face hardships. For example, data
showed that in several arts realms, the economy and employment
have not fully returned to pre-pandemic levels and arts
workers' wages have faced a slow recovery, and there is
tremendous need. Year after year, the number of applications to
the NEA continues to rise.
The President's budget meets this critical moment. It
supports and strengthens the NEA's core funding opportunities
and partnerships, bolsters the creative economy at a
significant time of need, expands opportunities for arts
participation for all, including arts education in rural,
Tribal, and other historically underserved communities, and
advances work at the intersection of arts and health and well-
being. Research demonstrates that the arts lead to positive
outcomes. For example, the arts are shown to improve social and
emotional learning amongst students. Arts participation
benefits brain health for seniors and helps address the
invisible wounds of war for veterans and active military and
more.
The President's budget reflects what we know to be true: we
cannot reach our full potential without access to the arts. The
budget continues to invest in initiatives with broad reach and
strong bipartisan support, such as Creative Forces, the Arts
Education Partnership, Poetry Out Loud, and Big Read. It
continues the longstanding practice of awarding 40 percent of
NEA's grantmaking dollars to State and jurisdictional arts
agencies and regional arts organizations for re-granting. From
Idaho Falls to Portland, Maine, local arts agencies extend the
reach and impact of Federal investments, and we are grateful
for these mutually beneficial partnerships.
The President's budget also bolsters our obligation and
commitment to serving indigenous communities, informed by
indigenous artists and leaders, who are making additional
investments to better serve, including hiring our first
director of Native Arts and Tribal Affairs. The Arts Endowment
is fortifying relationships with minority-serving institutions
of higher education, which provide valuable educational
opportunities, prepare new generations of workers and
entrepreneurs, and enrich the cultural fabric of our country.
We recognize the importance of engagement in rural
communities. Working with rural leaders, we are finding new
ways to help ensure longstanding programs have greater benefit.
For example, the Arts Endowment is strengthening its Citizens'
Institute on Rural Design, which has operated for decades, and
Shakespeare in American Communities now supports touring
programs with expanded geographic coverage in rural places.
Through these and other programs, this budget will allow us to
strengthen connection to rural America and support local
leaders. The President's budget supports ArtsHERE, a
partnership-based national initiative launched in fiscal year
2024, which bolsters programs and services for all types of
historically underserved communities.
We are also strengthening our work on arts and health. We
are proud of our longstanding record of supporting service
members, veterans, and military-connected populations, and we
continue to expand the Creative Forces program. We are also
building on a robust body of research that demonstrates
connection of the arts to our health and well-being. Consistent
with this progress, the President's budget will support a new
initiative focused on healing through the arts, emphasizing
mental health and recovery from trauma and disaster. This will
create new opportunities for arts workers to be fairly
compensated, while contributing to our well-being.
In closing, thank you again to Chairman Simpson, Ranking
Member Pingree, and to all members of the subcommittee, and I
am happy to take your questions.
[The statement of Ms. Jackson follows:]
[GRAPHIC(S) NOT AVAILABLE IN TIFF FORMAT]

Mr. Simpson. Thank you. Chair Lowe.
Ms. Lowe. Thank you so much, Chairman Simpson, Ranking
Member Pingree, and distinguished members of the subcommittee.
[Speaking native language.] My name is Shelly Lowe. I am
Navajo. I am originally from Coronado, Arizona, and I am chair
of the National Endowment for the Humanities. I just want to
thank you for the opportunity to provide testimony on the
President's fiscal year 2025 budget request for NEH.
NEH is the Nation's largest Federal funder of the
humanities, which includes history, philosophy, literature,
language, ethics, law, archaeology, political theory,
comparative religion, anthropology, sociology, and media and
cultural studies, which I knew you knew all of those. Since its
founding in 1965, NEH has awarded nearly $6 billion in grants
to support museums, historic sites, colleges, universities, K-
12 teachers, libraries, public television and radio stations,
research institutions, independent scholars, and State and
jurisdictional humanities councils nationwide.
NEH approaches its 60th anniversary in fiscal year 2025,
and now more than ever, NEH is vital to fostering a strong,
resilient, and thriving democracy, and to sustaining the United
States' role as a global leader in the humanities. NEH's fiscal
year 2025 appropriations request of $200.1 million reflects the
resources needed to continue NEH's support for American civil
society, helping us to advance research and development in the
humanities, examine the human condition, understand our
cultural heritage, foster mutual respect for diverse beliefs
and cultures, develop media and information literacy, and
promote civic education. The President's budget includes $60
million or approximately 40 percent of NEH's programmatic
funding for NEH's State affiliates, the State and
jurisdictional humanities councils, to support humanities
programming at the local level in all 50 States, the District
of Columbia, Puerto Rico, the U.S. Virgin Islands, Guam, the
Northern Mariana Islands, and American Samoa.
NEH's 56 State and jurisdictional humanities councils are
essential partners to ensuring the Agency reaches audiences of
all ages and in every community across the Nation. One example
is through NEH's longstanding partnership with National History
Day, which engages more than half a million middle and high
school students annually in a national historical research
competition. NEH recently launched a program with the
humanities councils to extend National History Day's reach to
underserved communities. Funded projects include support for a
2-day workshop for Idahoan teachers on primary source research
and place-based learning at the Idaho State Historical Society,
a free 3-day educator workshop in Georgia at the Nation's
largest consortium of historically black colleges and
universities to reach educators and students in underserved
urban communities in Atlanta and teachers in training at HBCUs,
and efforts to expand National History Day's impact in Maine in
the three most rural counties through partnerships with local
and Tribal educators as well as cultural community leaders.
NEH's fiscal year 2025 appropriations request also includes
$74.4 million for NEH's flagship grant programs to support
research and development, preservation, capacity building, and
educational and public programming in the humanities in all 56
States and jurisdictions. As the largest and most prestigious
funder of advanced humanities research in the United States,
NEH invests in scholarship and complex research projects by
individual scholars, collaborative research teams, and
institutions in all disciplines of the humanities and
humanistic social sciences.
While this work may sometimes seem distant from everyday
life, humanities' research and development, or R&D, is
foundational to American life. For example, NEH's support for
humanities' R&D has resulted in the publication of more than
16,000 books and academic monographs since NEH's founding in
1965, including 20 Pulitzer Prize-winning books. This includes
``American Prometheus: The Triumph and Tragedy of J. Robert
Oppenheimer'' by Kai Bird and Martin J. Sherwin, the biography
that inspired the Academy Award-winning film, ``Oppenheimer.''
NEH also supports the building blocks of humanities' R&D by
ensuring the public can more easily find and make use of
cultural heritage collections at libraries, archives, museums,
and historical organizations across the country, and by
fortifying under resourced collecting organizations, such as
small and midsized libraries, archives, and museums.
Another core aspect of NEH's work is its support for the
teaching and study of humanities at K-12 schools and colleges
and universities around the Nation, including Native-American
and indigenous institutions, historically black colleges and
universities, Hispanic-serving institutions, community
colleges, rural colleges and universities, schools with a
majority and minority undergraduate enrollment, and schools
with significant numbers of first generation students.
NEH's public programming grants support opportunities,
support the production of documentary films, museum
exhibitions, and discussion programs that engage public
audiences of all ages in questions with the humanities, such as
the ``Civil War,'' the landmark documentary series by Ken Burns
that was viewed by 4 million Americans when it first aired.
NEH's leadership role in digital humanities has been widely
acknowledged in the humanities field and has helped set the
pace for research funders around the world.
NEH's fiscal year 2025 appropriations request also provides
$8.75 million in Federal matching funds for NEH's grant
programs to help stimulate and match non-Federal donations in
support of cultural and educational institutions across the
nation. This includes support for a new pilot program that will
focus on workforce development through matching funds for the
hiring, training, and retention of humanities professionals at
museums, libraries, archives, colleges and universities, and
other cultural organizations across the country. The humanities
are more essential than ever.
The President's fiscal year 2025 budget for NEH provides
$14.6 million for the Agency's American Tapestry: Weaving
Together Past, Present, and Future strategic initiative, which
emphasizes the role of the humanities in strengthening our
democracy, advancing equity for all, and addressing our
changing climate. Of note, NEH's fiscal year 2025 appropriation
request includes $7.1 million for humanities perspectives on
artificial intelligence, NEH's new agency-wide research
initiative that supports humanities R&D to understand and
address the ethical, legal, and societal implications of
artificial intelligence or AI. AI is one of the most powerful
technologies of our time and will have profound consequences
for civil rights and civil liberties, safety and security, and
democratic values. Questions about the ethical, legal, and
societal implications of AI are fundamentally rooted in the
humanities.
NEH's founding legislation tasked the Agency with making
the American people masters of their technology and not its
unthinking servants. Created in response to President Biden's
Executive Order 14110 on safe, secure, and trustworthy
artificial intelligence, NEH's AI initiative features a new
grant program that supports the creation of humanities research
centers focused on AI at universities and independent research
organizations. NEH's appropriations request for fiscal year
2025 includes funding to include emphasis on equity, privacy,
and human rights, as well as educational and public programming
that enhance AI literacy and public awareness of human
perspectives on AI, such as support for the nationwide AI
literacy program with all 56 States and jurisdictional
humanities council.
NEH's fiscal year 2025 appropriations request also includes
$2.8 million to continue the Agency's activities as part of
United We Stand: Connecting Through Culture, an initiative
launched in partnership with NEA that aims to combat hate-
motivated violence, promotes civic engagement, and achieve
better mental health outcomes in the United States through the
arts and humanities. Since the launch of this initiative in
fiscal year 2022, NEH has invested more than $13 million to
support humanities projects that foster cross-cultural
understanding, empathy, and community resilience, and promote
civic engagement, information literacy, and social cohesion.
Furthermore, NEH properly contributed to the formation of the
White House's first ever U.S. National Strategy to counter
Antisemitism through commitments to offer research, teaching,
and convening opportunities for humanities scholars to study
the origins and history of antisemitism in the United States,
as well as support the development of K-12 educational
materials on countering antisemitism in partnership with
National History Day and the U.S. Holocaust Memorial Museum.
NEH will continue to support these activities in fiscal year
2025.
The President's fiscal year 2025 budget for NEH also
includes $1.5 million for the development of a new program to
support the revitalization and reclamation of native languages
and cultures. This program will complement NEH's partnership
with the Department of the Interior on the Federal Indian
Boarding School Initiative, a comprehensive effort to recognize
the troubled legacy of Federal Indian boarding school policies,
with the goal of addressing their intergenerational impact and
shedding light on the traumas of the past. To date, NEH has
allocated nearly $5 million to support the digitization of
records from the United States' system of over 400 Federal
Indian boarding schools, the creation of a permanent oral
history collection to document the experiences of the
generations of indigenous students who were raised in the
Federal Indian boarding school system, and new humanities
projects that deepen the public's understanding of this chapter
in our Nation's history.
NEH's fiscal year 2025 appropriations request will also
further the Pacific Islands Cultural Initiative, an NEH
initiative that aims to strengthen the cultural and educational
sectors in the U.S. Pacific islands, Hawaii, American Samoa,
Guam, and the Northern Mariana Islands, which face unique
challenges due to their remote geography and the historic
underinvestment in the region by the Federal Government. NEH's
work in the U.S. Pacific islands includes emergency relief for
cultural and educational organizations devastated by the
wildfires in Maui in 2023. NEH's recent disaster recovery
efforts also includes support for cultural organizations
affected by natural disasters in Florida, Mississippi, New
Mexico, and Vermont.
Finally, the President's budget for fiscal year 2025
provides $42.35 million to operate NEH in an efficient and
effective manner. This includes dedicated resources for two new
offices formed as part of NEH's equity action plan to embed
equity across its programs and operations, the Office of Data
and Evaluation and the Office of Outreach, which will evaluate
NEH's impact on the humanities field and deepen the Agency's
engagement with underserved communities. The budget also
supports the continued implementation of executive orders,
directives, and administrative priorities involving
organizational health and organizational performance and
information security modernization.
Chairman Simpson, Ranking Member Pingree, distinguished
members of the committee, thank you again for supporting NEH
and for your consideration of the Agency's fiscal year 2025
appropriations request.

Mr. Simpson. Thank you. Thank you all for your testimony.
Your full testimony will be included in the record. We have had
hearings in the last few days on things like the EPA and the
Forest Service and the Department of Interior and Indian Health
Services and all those things that we need to do and like to do
or are required to do. These are kind of things we like to do,
so I am glad you are here today and to be able to talk about
the budget requests.
Director Feldman, this past year, the National Gallery lent
397 artworks to 43 museums across the United States. This
partnership allows Americans across the country to see some of
the Gallery's collection and original works without necessarily
having to travel to Washington. How have you utilized the
Gallery's lending program to expand the Gallery's reach and
impact, and what plans do you have to expand this practice to
additional museums? What are the challenges to bringing them to
more rural America, and have you been able to partner with
museums in smaller communities in rural areas?
Ms. Feldman. Thank you very much, Chair Simpson. We take
our role serving the Nation very seriously. I like to say that
I was hired with one very firm mandate from our Board of
Trustees at the Gallery, which is to expand our service to the
American people. I never speak to our staff at the museum
without reminding them all that we have an obligation and also
a privilege to serve the American people. We spend a lot of our
time thinking about how to expand that service.
As you mentioned, we are very, very generous, lending works
of art from our collection for exhibitions that travel around
the country and globally as well. Our answer is always yes when
we are asked for a loan, unless there is some reason why that
work of art, usually, because of condition or other
commitments, can't travel. We do a lot of that. We are in
discussions right now about really expanding our loan program
and lending works from the collection to smaller institutions
that serve their regions and doing it with long-term loans. It
is not just a 3-month exhibition, but actually enabling the
national collection to be in communities for a couple of years,
and also doing that with exhibitions.
I think I have a relatively unique position as the director
of the National Gallery, particularly, according to my
predecessors, because I have run really small museums across
the country. This is my fourth museum that I have directed, and
I often say that I know what it is like to have to go out and
raise money for a lawnmower because one broke and there just
isn't a budget to replace it. I know what the challenge is for
our institutions around the country, and we are dedicated to
serving them. We do it with all humility. We recognize that we
have as much to learn from our regional partners as we have to
share, so we are really excited about expanding that.
In addition, if you will indulge me, put together a list of
some of the other things we do to serve the American people who
maybe don't necessarily come to Washington. Although 50 percent
of the National Gallery's attendance comes from domestic
tourists from outside of our region. Through visitation we
serve the Nation, and we are really proud that we, over the
last year, served close to 20 million people through our
website and our social media programs. With our website and
those programs, of course, we are focused on art education and
human creativity, and so we use our collection as the vehicle
in all of those systems that we distribute through the website.
We have a Summer Teacher Institute that last year reached
teachers from 27 different States, so we bring them here to
Washington. We are pausing this year so that we can figure out
how we can actually do more to impact rural teachers in the
future. We share traveling exhibitions. We have a show that
just opened in New Britain, Connecticut of contemporary Native-
American art. We are also working with Jackson, Mississippi,
Portland, Oregon, Denver, and Milwaukee in the coming year,
year and a half. We have over 25,000 participants who join us
for our virtual lectures, educational programs, programs for
children and parents, and our art activities.
I mentioned that we have a MOOC that has reached nearly
37,000 participants from every single State. We have something
at the National Gallery of Art called the Center for Advanced
Study in the Visual Arts. I always liken it to having a sort of
educational institute within the National Gallery. We bring a
series of scholars and fellows to the National Gallery and to
Washington. I am proud to say that we have alumni in 39
different States right now from that program. We also
distribute films that we have created about artists for
exhibitions over the last number of years to local affiliate TV
stations around the country. We had 49 million views of those
films over the last year, 12,000 schools receiving our
materials, with 125,000 students with access to posters and our
print materials in 42 different States. This year we had a boom
in applications for our internship program. We have a paid
internship program, and we had applications from 43 different
States.
Then the last thing I will mention is a new series on our
website we have started that is called From West to East. With
that series, we are highlighting institutions, communities, and
artists working all across the country and showing that, again,
our role as the national museum is to highlight the great
artists and creatives and institutions across America and not
just what we have in Washington. That is, I am sorry, a very
long answer to your question, but we are very proud.

Ms. Feldman. The Gallery has three Leonardo da Vinci works,

did around 1490. He would walk the streets of Florence and
Milan where he lived and come back and make these drawings.
These grotesque figures were not people that he saw
necessarily. They come out of his imagination. They are sort of
studies that prepare him, and I have to point out one of my
favorite things. In addition to her sort of grotesque face, you
can see how her cheeks are sunken in because she has lost her
teeth. With all of that, she has a little carnation tucked into
her breast that is just peeking out there, so you get that
sense of sort of liveliness that Leonardo is so famous for.

Ms. Jackson. Thank you for the question, Ranking Member
Pingree, and, yes, it was wonderful to be in Maine with you. I

The work at the Arts Endowment, I think, right now is

programs, the longstanding programs, and ensuring that they are
truly reaching all Americans, that they are truly benefiting

I am very excited about the work that we are doing to be

relationships and stronger impact in communities that have been
underserved, including rural, Tribal communities, with
disability communities that have been historically marginalized
as a result of geography, race, socioeconomic status, et
cetera. The work that is focused on the intersection of arts
and health and well-being is something that is also really
exciting and builds on the Creative Forces work. It builds on
research that is gaining momentum that is showing all kinds of
value and impacts of the arts, active arts participation as
well as engagement as audience, having to do with the social
fabric, with social cohesion, mental health.
Making sure that we are able to talk about the arts in
their fullness and how, at their best, they contribute, not in
a silo or a bubble, but throughout people's lives in all
different kinds of communities. The funding has allowed us to
make progress and make sure that we are meeting mission in
considering the context and times now.
Ms. Pingree. Thank you. Thank you so much. I might as well
pick on you, too, Chairwoman Lowe. Thank you. I am looking
forward to getting you to Maine as well, and thank you for your
very comprehensive overview of all the work that you do at the
National Endowment for the Humanities. It is such a critically
important part of what we do.
One of the things I am excited about that you are doing--I
really don't want to single anything out because I think it is
all so good--but I just want to talk a little bit about the
American Tapestry initiative. I was really pleased when earlier
this year the Penobscot Nation Museum and the Hudson Museum at
the University of Maine received a significant NEH grant to
create a visual directory of Wabanaki basketry. We are very
proud of our basket makers in Maine. We are really excited to
see increasing recognition for artists like Jeremy Frey, Teresa
Secord. There was just a great story in The New York Times
about how basket makers are sort of moving from a obscure craft
to, you know, front and center art, and the Portland Museum of
Art will be hosting an exhibit of Jeremy Frey.
This grant will fund the creation of an online lexicon with
images and terminology, and the work will help reduce errors in
identification and preserve ancestral knowledge. I think it is
an excellent example of preserving our heritage. Can you just
tell us a little more about the American Tapestry initiative
and how you have been able to use the increases that the NEH
received for programs like this over the last few years?
Ms. Lowe. Definitely. Thank you so much for that question,
Ranking Member Pingree, and my understanding is I have Maine on
my schedule to visit before the end of the year. I will be very
much looking forward to it because I have been to the Native-
American Maine basket maker show and sale, and there is nothing
quite like seeing the work that the artists put into everything
that they bring forward and the culture that they share.
American Tapestry: Weaving Together Past, Present, and
Future was an initiative created with joint effort from all of
our program directors to really assess the work of the Agency
and to think about ways that we can extend our work into those
areas and communities that we had not already been reaching. We
focused on three major areas: strengthening our democracy,
equity for all, and addressing our changing climate. In this,
our program directors really thought very hard about what we
were funding now, what grant opportunities we had available,
and how we could extend that to ensure that we really were
reaching all communities and all individuals across the United
States and bringing humanities to all.
For instance, in some of our equity work, it meant
developing new grant lines to allow smaller organizations to
apply with projects that are a little bit less costly. The
funding would be lower. The management of those funds would be
a little bit easier. This is something that we learned through
the pandemic with our CARES and ARP funding, that some smaller
organizations that we had never worked with before started to
receive funding from us through CARES and ARP, and the process
that we were able to go through to support them, including
reporting lines that were not as extensive as when you have
very large grants. This was a very encouraging sign for us to
be able to say that we could do some of our grant lines,
specifically for smaller communities, organizations, but also
to bring forward those stories that we haven't heard from
before or those organizations and communities that were doing
fabulous and amazing work in States and regions but were not
getting funding at the national level.
Like the example that you shared, in addition to other work
with small communities to really bring their culture forward,
to be able to share that amongst themselves, but to share it
more locally in their regions and then to share it nationally.
We have been very excited about how well this has been received
by communities across the States and across the country, and to
how many organizations and community members are coming to us
now and saying, you know, I am really excited NEH is actually
doing something that supports the work that we do and can help
us to get this work moving forward.
We will continue to do that work. We will continue to focus
on strengthening democracy through the National History Day,
like I mentioned, which was really an effort on our part to
ensure that those communities that were not yet participating
in National History Day, maybe hadn't even really known about
it. We are having resources and contacts that could help them
to reach those students and teachers and communities and bring
them into this national competition and national conversation.
Of course, to think about how we are looking at artificial
intelligence, not just at the national level, but also at the
local level, what do we do to support that and to have these
conversations about, what it means for our future as human
beings as we move forward.
Lastly, I would just like to say the climate aspect of the
initiative also reaches those smaller communities and
organizations who have really been able to assess their own
resources in terms of how they are preserving their cultural
heritage, especially in small organizations and communities
that are in older buildings and are having to say, you know,
our building is not going to be able to preserve this material.
We need to be able to think about how we make some changes,
start some plans, and move that forward. We have provided the
opportunity for organizations to be able to do that. We are
very excited and we are looking forward to seeing how we can
continue to fund organizations through this initiative.

Ms. Jackson. Happy to take those questions. Thank you.
First off, you know, the focus on rural communities is
something that we have been very serious about and intentional
about in the last few years, and understanding through
proximity to these communities what they actually need seems
really important, as Congresswoman Pingree recounted the site
visit that we did. Getting close and talking with leaders in
communities and understanding how their cultural ecosystems may
look different from others and being able to be responsive to
that is really important.
We are looking at all of our programs to understand how can
they better show up with communities that require additional
attention and require particular understanding of how they
work. The Citizens Institute on Rural Design, for example, is a
program that we are expanding. I am especially excited about
how that expansion happens because it builds capacity locally.
It builds local leaders and helps them to understand the
utility of design methods and the importance of the arts in
their communities on their terms, not in a way that is
helicoptered in, but in a way that feels organic to what they
have to offer and what they aspire to. There is that kind of
review and adjustments that are happening throughout the
agency.
I think in terms of working with our military community,
certainly Creative Forces is something that we have so much
pride and respect for. We are really excited about the
expansion of that program to now not only serve in clinical
settings and on military sites, but in communities. As military
personnel go back into communities and civilian life, there is
an opportunity to continue to benefit from the kinds of
experiences that allow them to heal. Creative Forces has
adjacent to it some research programs that help us understand
the benefits of both individually and collectively, and that is
something that I think is beneficial not only for the military
community, but for the whole country to understand how health
can be improved, how well-being can be improved. Certainly
paying attention to the military population as a first starting
place is the right thing to do, and there are so many benefits
that flow from that and we are eager to capture those.
Mr. Ellzey. Well said. Ms. Lowe, when you opened up, I
assume, in your native tongue, I have a question about the
preservation of indigenous languages and how we are losing
those at a very quick rate. How is the Endowment for the
Humanities working to preserve our native languages?
Ms. Lowe. Thank you for that question. We have had two
longstanding grant lines, one in our Research Division that is
a collaboration with the National Science Foundation to
document endangered languages. These are large grants for
researchers who come in and really look at the lexicon and the
makeup of languages to document them and to put them into
writing to preserve them. We also have a grant line through our
Preservation and Access Division that allows communities to do
grants that create things like dictionaries and online lexicons
or online resources for community members. I was recently in
Mississippi at the Mississippi Choctaw Nation, and I met the
current Ms. Choctaw, and she talked about how she uses the
online Choctaw dictionary in her work, especially when she goes
out and she speaks to communities. It is a resource that now
students can get online and they can look up words. They are
starting to learn the language, but they can at least look up
words and figure out, you know, what is grandma or grandpa
saying, what are they talking about, right? All of those things
that they hadn't known before.
We have been really focused on making sure that we are
supporting communities to revitalize and document their
languages. In our 2025 budget, we have got 1.5 million
earmarked to really support this work, and this follows up with
our work with the Department of the Interior on the Federal
Indian Boarding School Initiative.
Mr. Ellzey. Mr. Chairman, I yield back.

Ms. Lowe. Yeah.
Ms. McCollum. I noticed in your full statement, you talk

Ms. Lowe. Thank you so much for that question. Well, we
began this work specifically on AI under the American Tapestry
Initiative with a grant called Dangers and Opportunities of
Technology through our Office of Digital Humanities. This was
to take projects specifically from researchers, independent
scholars, teams within institutions to really assess what are
the dangers, but also what are the opportunities of technology
so that we have a better sense ourselves as a national
community where technology does great things for us, but also
where we need to be watching out for dangers of technology.
This has grown into a new initiative that we are calling
Humanities Research Centers for Artificial Intelligence, and
this is specifically to look at AI, artificial intelligence, as
it is being used across our society.
This will be a collaboration between university,
institutions, or between researchers within institutions or
independent research organizations to look at the topic, to
really delve into it and to think about how do we assess our
ethical, legal, societal concerns when it comes to our use of
artificial intelligence. The information and research that will
come out of this will help us to create information that our
State and jurisdictional humanities councils can create
training programs under, that can create educational materials
under. This will be material that can be used in the future to
really think about how we educate ourselves and how we inform
ourselves about artificial intelligence and our use of
artificial intelligence.

first time a director of Native Arts and Tribal Affairs. The

insightful and knowledgeable about how best to serve Native
populations, recognizing that there is history and complexity,
and we want to be able to meet that need, and as well as not
only meet needs, but fuel aspirations. It is wonderful to see
not only the reclamation of culture, but the advancement of
living culture.
To the previous point about, you know, where does art and
culture live, it lives in all communities. The Arts Endowment's
role is to help to bolster the cultural ecosystems that allow
for all people to deliver their cultural gifts to the country.
I think these are examples of places where intention and
attention is necessary, and I am happy that we are able to
provide that.
Mr. Kilmer. Excellent. Thank you for that. Chair Lowe, I

Reading Program, which helps families bond and develop a
relationship with reading and books. We actually got a
statement from the Pierce County Library System that said,
``Students in the Primetime Family Reading Program see
themselves in readers and lovers of books. They are beginning
to read more fluently and more often. What more could we ask
for?'' Each session includes a complimentary meal and
storytelling, and Humanities Washington has now expanded the
program to include preschool age studies as well. To date, they
have served over 10,000 individuals and nearly 3,500 families
and only made possible because of grants from the National
Endowment for the Humanities. I am very grateful for your

Ms. Lowe. Yes. Thank you for that question, and thank you
for acknowledging Humanities Washington. Our State and
jurisdictional humanities councils in all 56 States and
jurisdictions do amazing work on the ground, with local
communities addressing the needs that are very specific to
their States and the populations of their States. That
particular program is just one example of a literacy program
that is being offered and run by one of the State and
jurisdictional councils. We have a number of them, of the
councils that do these programs that are very specific to
connecting teaching literacy to young children, but also
teaching literacy to the adults that are in their household who
maybe are not fluent English speakers so that they can have
literacy within the home to support the youth who are going to
school. The other avenues that we support literacy projects are
through funding to libraries, both State libraries and local
libraries, many of them that do their own literacy programs for
various populations across their regions and across their
communities.
We also do major funding. We did a grant to the American
Library Association for Great Stories Club, reading and
discussions for at-risk youth in particular. These are more
national programs that really bring literacy projects and
programs to individuals that might not get them otherwise, and
we are very proud of this work and really interested in
supporting this kind of work across the country.
Mr. Kilmer. Super. Chairman, do I have time for one more?
Okay. I am moving from reading to writing. I appreciated that
the NEH intends to carry out a program model on the original
Federal Writers' Project, which was outlined in the budget
justification. The original Federal Writers' Project produced
books describing American life in 48 States, 40 cities, 18
regions and territories, provided the curation of invaluable
oral histories, including an accounting of more than 2,300

as we approach the 250th anniversary of our country.
Ms. Lowe. Sure. Thank you so much for that question as
well. I will have to say that NEH, you know, one of the visions
that we have and the mission that we have is in 250 years, when
Americans have a question about who we were 250 years ago, the
material to answer those questions will be available and they
will have access to it, and that is exactly the type of
projects that we fund.
We have already been doing some of this work through our
Preservation and Access Division, supporting communities who
are taking oral histories and written documents from
individuals who are talking about their experiences over the
pandemic and dealing with COVID-19. We have a model for
starting this program and for really thinking about how this
work will move forward. We have also supported some of this
work at our State and jurisdictional humanities councils,
working very specifically in small communities, particularly
those communities that unfortunately lost a number of their
community members to COVID-19. We are very interested in
continuing this work. We are actively looking at what that
project will look like as we go into celebrating the 250th, and
we are delighted to be able to support it.

Mr. Edwards. Thank you. All right. Well, thanks all of you
for being here with us this afternoon.
Dr. Rosario, going through your testimony and the
President's budget request for fiscal year 2025, I appreciate
that the request for funding increases are relatively minor. As
we all know, like many American families, we are all having to
do more with less in the most recent years. During the fiscal
year 2024 appropriations cycle, I voted on an amendment that
would have cut all the funding for both the National Endowments
for the Arts and Humanities, the NEA and NEH. I voted against
the amendment that would have cut all of that. I voted no, and
I am confident that there are a lot of folks back in my
constituency that disagree with that vote.
My request to you or question to you now is simple, and I
open it up to anyone that would like to take a shot at
answering it. Can you explain why the NEA and NEH are deserving
of hundreds of millions of dollars of hard-earned taxpayers'
money each year and why my colleagues and I should oppose
efforts to strip such funding, as I am sure that we will most
likely have to take that vote again this year?
Ms. Jackson. Thank you. Thank you for your support and
thank you for the question. I think that the most comprehensive
answer is that the arts are foundational to all that we want to
achieve as a Nation of opportunity and justice. The arts allow
us to be fully human, to express our humanity, to see the
humanity of others, and I think that in and of itself is a
precondition for all of the other things that we say we want to
accomplish. We can get more specific and talk about the impacts
of the arts on the economy and recognize that it is an
important economic driver. Increasingly, research tells us that
it has a lot to do with our well-being and our health. It is
part of what makes for a viable democracy. It is essential to
our social fabric and to re-knitting that social fabric.
I think that more robust understanding of the arts and
humanities is the kind of understanding that is necessary for
us to recognize that this is one of our wisest investments in
our country.
Mr. Edwards. I appreciate that response. There are a lot of
folks back at home that would not agree with that since we have
so many other priorities in America right now, so I am sure
this committee is going to be faced with another such vote. My
next question is for you, Ms. Lowe, and, again, I appreciate
you being with us today. I know that colleges and universities
benefit every year from NEH grants to support humanities
education, including those in my own district. We know that in
recent days, we have seen some institutions like Columbia in
New York City and George Washington University just up the road
become horrific epicenters of antisemitism in the guise of
protests against the war between Israel and Hamas terrorists.
Do you believe, and if so why, that institutions that fail to
make Jewish students or any culture or race feel safe to go to
school and rally against different types of racism should be
eligible for NEH grant funding?
Ms. Lowe. Thank you so much, Committee Member Edwards, for
that question, and I am like most Americans. I am very aware of
what has been occurring on college campuses, and I think there
are a number of important American values that are at stake in
what has been happening, one, the right to attend a college or
university free from unlawful discrimination, the right to free
speech, and the right to assemble and protest peacefully.
With that being said, I am not in a position to be able to
comment on the events or incidents as they are sensitive and
fact-specific matters that are addressed best by college and
university leaders where they are taking place on their
campuses. What I can assure the committee members today is that
in my role as chair of the National Endowment for the
Humanities, I take very seriously the full enforcement of the
vast body of Congress' antidiscrimination laws, and that
includes Title VI, Title IX, and Section 504 of the
Rehabilitation Act of 1975. In the event that NEH may receive
reports that a recipient of our funds has or is violating any
of Congress's nondiscrimination laws, we will act very quickly
to investigate, and if we find that discrimination has
occurred, we will terminate the funding to that recipient and,
if appropriate, refer the matter to the Department of Justice
for corrective litigation.
With that being said, our grant opportunities are open to
institutions across the United States. They are reviewed under
a very rigorous peer review process. They are awarded based on
the strengths of their applications and what has been
presented, but we take very seriously if the institutions are
doing something that is a violation of the law.
Mr. Edwards. Thank you. I appreciate your candid answers,
and, Mr. Chair, I yield.
Mr. Simpson. Thank you. Let me ask a question that is kind

Ms. Lowe. One of the things that we are very focused on is
civic education, particularly among young people. This is part
of the work that we are doing with National History Day and
supporting that work across all 56 State and jurisdictions, and
bringing students into that work to help them really understand
what it means to be a part of a democracy, a very strong
democracy. Where did that democracy come from? What informs

among their peers, and then share it on the national stage in a

have. We are very, very interested in supporting that work and
continuing that.

director. I know you have really thought a lot about diversity
and inclusion in your exhibit. I just want to hear a little bit
from you about how your perspective now on, you know, what
should be included in museums and galleries has influenced the
Gallery's practices and the collections and how some of the
things you have been thinking are to sort of better reflect

I will just editorialize a bit. Actually, when Chair
Jackson came to me, we took her to a couple of different
exhibits. I thought it was interesting to see traditional
museums, very small museums, but still saying, no, we are not
going to just put the kind of old masters of our collection.
You know, how do we lead with things that are about indigenous
people and the multiplicity of cultures that live in our State,
or even talking about some of the tougher issues that we need
to address through art. Anyway, I am interested, not to lead
you into a complicated issue. You know, your perspective is
different. You are the first female director, and have you been
thinking about this and what goes into the collection?
Ms. Feldman. Thank you, Ms. Pingree. Actually, I often tell
a story that, when I was contemplating the opportunity at the
National Gallery, I had breakfast with one of my mentors in
Minneapolis, Richard Davis. I told him that I had only ever
worked for museums that were deeply embedded in a community and
that were responsive to their community, and I didn't know how
to approach a Federal institution. Richard leaned across and he
said, yes, but the Nation becomes your community, and what does
that mean? I immediately got excited about that and realized it
was a new challenge for me. After arriving at the Gallery, we
drafted a vision statement, which says, ``Of the Nation and for
all the people,'' and that really is what drives our work. We
think constantly about the plurality that is America and how we
are sure that we are an institution that enables people to find
themselves broadly. There isn't one answer to that.
We love our collections and our historic collections, and
we are thinking about how we do represent the Nation. For
example, when I arrived, the museum didn't have any major works
by Native-American artists, and we are the National Gallery of
Art. We have added the acquisition of works by Native-American
artists to the work that we are doing at the Gallery and just
trying to be a place where all people can find themselves. By
diversifying the staff and our program, we have been able to do
that but not at the detriment of any one group. We really are
here to serve all people and trying to tell new stories with
the collection.
For example, we recently acquired a painting by an artist,
Mattia Preti, who was an Italian artist from the late 17th
century. It is not a portrait, but it shows probably a real man
who was sitting on the ground. It was painted when the artist
was in Malta in the late 17th century, and the man is sitting
on the ground and he is cutting tobacco. Most of our Italian
pictures are of religious subjects, and this painting enables
people to see somebody who was perhaps enslaved at the time
because of where Malta was located. Certainly, a working class
person, and he is cutting tobacco. It also tells the story of
tobacco coming into Europe in the 17th century, and it is a
fantastic work of art that meets all of our requirements of
being an important work. We are trying to tell new stories so
that we, you know, broaden exposure and the attraction to the
public.
Ms. Pingree. Thank you for that. Thank you, Mr. Chair.

Mr. Ellzey. No.

Ms. Feldman. Thank you for that question, Ms. McCollum. We
recently agreed to lend a work of art to a small regional
museum, and the director, who I know quite well, sent me a
letter and said that he found our requirements for transport
and crating onerous. In his letter he said we small
institutions are not going to survive if you big ones don't
help us out. I was really struck by his comment, and as I said
earlier, from my past experience, I know what that is like.
Much of our work now at the National Gallery is thinking
about how can we leverage our collection, our resources,
support from our patrons so that we can help because it is not
just lending the works of art to these institutions. They need
help with shipping and insurance and security officers,
education programs, and so we are taking a really long look at
that because I do feel that we have, as the National Gallery,
the obligation and opportunity to serve regional institutions.
Ms. Jackson. Thank you for the question. As I said earlier
today, we are taking very seriously the charge of reducing
barriers as much as we possibly can. We know that there are
many viable and stretched organizations that are important to
communities around the country. Being able to, I think, do two
things, work with them to remove some of the barriers, whether
it is through webinars or technical assistance in terms of
filling out grant requirements and similar, that is something
that we are committed to, but also taking a look at what can we
do differently that actually removes barriers to organizations
that, frankly, we have to rely on. We need to understand their
importance in helping us meet mission. The notion of removing
barriers, I think, isn't only about how can we help
organizations meet the match, do the paperwork, et cetera, but
can we also look internally to see if there are things that we
can change.
I know that the staff is really committed to being an ally
in helping people apply. When we go around the country, one of
the things that I am very proud of is that people often say
that when they engage with staff that they have had good
experiences, and I hope to continue to promote that. Certainly
working with the small organizations and getting to a place
where we can do that easily is something that we also I think
need to work with State arts agencies of local arts agencies
on. That coordination at the different levels to relieve
barriers is something that we are taking seriously.
Ms. Lowe. Yes, and thank you for pointing that out, and
kind of some of the challenges that we see in entities that are
trying to apply to us, we fund nonprofits. We have an Office of
Challenge Programs that is specifically built to help leverage
non-Federal funding for projects. We find that applicants who
apply to NEH who are successful, who get an award from NEH,
will often say, you know, this allowed us to apply for grants
from other organizations, private foundations, and funders
because they see that an award from NEH is a seal of approval
that we are doing great work. We have such a rigorous peer-
review process and we, you know, fund on the strengths of the
applications, they have been able then to say we can take this
funding from NEH and tell a private funder we have got this
money, and say, okay, we are really interested in the work that
you are doing, you know, is there something that we can do to
help? That is what our Office of Challenge Programs is set up
to help organizations do. They are required to do fundraising,
to do some cost sharing, cost matching for the amount that they
are looking for.
We have also had the opportunity in fiscal year 2024 to
fill our assistant chair for strategic partnerships and
initiatives, which is a position that had been vacant for a
number of years. With this individual in place, we have been
able to really start working on developing conversations and
partnerships with private funders to inform them about the work
that our applicants are doing, the needs that are still in
those areas. Within those either regions, communities, or in
those specific topical areas, that private funding could then
come in and help support. They have been very interested in the
work that we are doing, the topical areas that we are
supporting, and are very interested in having conversations and
understanding what is the best way that they can also support
the work that these communities are doing.
This also goes to our State and jurisdictional humanities
councils, who serve oftentimes as kind of a capacity-building
entity for these organizations, particularly small ones, to
connect them to other funding opportunities, to fund them
themselves and sometimes with smaller amounts, but to make
those connections to ensure that we have the opportunity to
bring more funding into these organizations.

Ms. Lowe. You mentioned the State and jurisdictional

jurisdictional humanities councils also often serve as centers
for the book in each State. You mentioned, you know, literacy.
Part of talking about civility and talking about the ability to
speak with other individuals is reading books, is reading books
that are picked by the States, and they will do a book for
adults. They will do a nonfiction. They will do a book for
young readers. They will pass these books out. They will make
them available to communities. They will bring in authors. They
will have discussion series. A lot of them will do book
festivals, where, you know, parents come with their children,
hundreds of people come to see the books, to get copies of the
books, just like we do here at the National Book Festival.
Then to also have these really in-depth conversation with
authors, who come from across the country to have these
conversations to talk about the resources that they provide
through their books. These individuals, families can walk away
with that and can walk away with some ideas on how to think
about what they have read, how to think about the discussions
that they have had. I mean, we have seen just great success
with book festivals across the country.
Mr. Kilmer. Great. Thank you.
Ms. Feldman. I will just throw in that I think that your
question is really another way of asking Mr. Edwards' question,
which was why the arts matter.
Mr. Kilmer. Yes.
Ms. Feldman. I go back to saying that it is about bringing
people together. It is about feeling that you are part of
something bigger than yourself. It has been proven by social
scientists that when people gather together and feel some kind
of awe in the presence of a work of art or a performance, that
they become less narcissistic. They stop checking their phone
and worrying about other things. They become more generous,
more conscious that they are part of something bigger than
themselves. What happens so often with the work that all three
of us do is that you are bringing together people who may not
normally be together. That is best way to break down barriers,
is when you can see another human being as a human being with
shared thoughts and ideas and create empathy for one another.
Mr. Kilmer. Yeah.
Ms. Feldman. I think that is why this work matters and why
we need to make sure that it is a backbone of what we do in the
United States.
Mr. Kilmer. Thank you. Maybe you will work with us to
organize a field trip of members of Congress. Come do that.
Ms. Feldman. I would love to.
Mr. Kilmer. Great. Thank you, Chairman. I yield back.
Mr. Simpson. Thank you, and thank you all for being here.
Thanks for bringing the toys.
Ms. Feldman. I am not going to tell our curator that you
called Leonardo a toy, though.
Mr. Simpson. I have got one final question. If you could
just answer it, Director Feldman. The fiscal year 2025 request
includes $7.7 million and 91 FTEs for guard contract security,
which is an increase of 21 FTEs over last year. I also
understand that of all your staff vacancies, contract security
guards are the highest percentage of vacancies across all job
functions. With the rising incidents of protesters defacing and
ruining precious works of art around the world, has the Gallery
changed its security posture and protocols to better protect
the collection, and have you seen an uptick in security
incidents, and did that prompt the increase in the budget
request for security guards?
Ms. Feldman. Thank you very much, Chair Simpson. Yes, one
of the reasons we are requesting more guards is because we have
been, thanks to your support, fixing the roof in several
quadrants of the West Building, which means galleries have been
closed, and we haven't needed as many officers because of those
closed galleries. We will be finishing that work and we want to
open up all of our galleries for our visitors, so it is partly
that. Yes, it is also the fact that we used to focus more on
the magnetometers at the front door looking for guns and knives
and metal, and we continue to do that. You would be surprised
how many guns and knives we pull off people every year.
What has changed now are the activists who are coming with
paint and food and substances to throw at works of art, or they
are hard to find, which means more bag searches and longer
lines. As all of you know, the security environment just keeps
changing for us, and we are determined to protect the people
who come to the museum and the people who work at the museum
and volunteer, and also these incredible works of art that we
hold in trust.
Mr. Simpson. Thank you. I appreciate it, and, again, thank
you all for being here. I love the NEH and I love the NEA and
the work that you guys do, and I will make sure that we contact
you so that we can set up a thing for the members and staff to
come over and visit because I really don't want to defund you.
I appreciate your willingness to do that.
Thank you all for being here today, and with that, this
committee stands adjourned.
